CONTRACTORS
DIFFER OVER
PAGO-PAGO
Wide Margin in the Eight
Tenders Furnished.
SEVEN LOCAL FIRMS 810
NO AWARD TO BE MADE TTNTIB
DEPARTMENT APPROVES.
Deposits Were Not Required With
the Tenders, So That No Firm
Is Compelled to Carry
Out the Work.
Local contractors differ remarkably tn
their estimate of the cost of constructing
a coaling station for Uncle Sam on Pago-
Pago harbor In Samoa. When Commander
Tanner opened the tenders at noon yes
terday he found that each of the eight
firms to which he had sent out plans and
specifications had furnished a bid. But
the difference In the estimates was enor
mous. The lowest tenderers, Messrs.
Cotton Bros. & Co. of Oakland, profess
themselves willing to erect the coaling
sheds, wharf, etc., for the trifling sum of.
$133,397. The Rtsdon Iron Works, on tha
other hand, consider that the same serv
ice should be compensated by no less than
$255 .000. There is thus a margin of $122,000
between the estimates. The intermediate
bids are: Joseph McMahon, $162,000; Cali
fornia Bridge Company, $154,00j; Ely, Tib
bits & Co., $142,000; King Bridge Com
pany, Cleveland, Ohio, $217,000; City Im
provement Company, $207,000; San Fran
cisco Bridge Company, $164,300.
Commander Tanner cannot at present
give any Indication of the final award.
"You know," he said, "I have first to sub
mit the name of the tenderer to Washing
ton in order to ascertain If the firm
stands well with the department. And
since the excitement of the war rush haa
passed most of the Federal officials are
away taking a much-needed holiday. So
there may be some little delay."
A peculiar feature about this contract
is that, contrary to the usual custom, noi
deposits were required with the tenders.
Commander Tanner did this In order to
expedite matters and avoid red tape.
Consequently none of the firms who have
put in estimates are obliged to undertake
the work, unless they so choose. Hence
the final acceptance of the contract may
be delayed, commander Tanner has sub
mitted the name of the lowest tenderer to
Washington, and if the reply is satisfac
tory we will ask the firm to undertake
the work. If It declines to sjgn the con
tract, the next highest on the list mugt
be applied to, and so on, until a practi
cal bid is reached.
Thus it is impossible to say which of the
eight companies which have sent in offers
will carry out the work. Seven out of the
eight firms belong to California, so that
there Is a fair chance of the work being
dene on this coast. In any case the ma
terial will have to be shipped from here
and the skilled labor required engaged in
this city.
Commander Tanner does not consider
that it will be necessary to have a United
States man-of-war at Pago-Pago harbor
during the work of construction, which
will probably occupy over a year. But
It might be advantageous as a method of
keeping the natives in order and asserting
Uncle Sam's prestige.
"Now that the war is over," he pointed
out, "probably the usual round of naval
visits will be resumed, and undoubtedly
one of our war ships will visit Samoa. The
Bennington has gone to Honolulu, but if
she should get news of Importance by the
Australian mails she might go on to the
Samoan group."
ANGRY MILKMEN.
Leipsic Brothers Sued the Board of
Health for Big Damages.
Leipsic Brothers, milk dealers, filed suit
yesterday against J. P. Dockery, Milk In
spectcr, and the Board of Health, to re
cover J25.000 damages, alleged to hays
been sustained by reason of statements
given out that the plaintiffs sold Impure
milk, and that the arrest of Isaac Leipslo
for selling adulterated milk was malicious
and groundless. The plaintiffs In their
complaint allege that Dockery Is a grossly
and notoriously incompetent man in the
business of inspecting and testing milk
and cream, and does not and never aid un
derstand the first rudiments of the busi
ness. "He has never had the proper or
any schooling sufficient to fit him for the
business," states the complaint. "He has
been frequently intoxicated while on duty
and shows favoritism to many milkmen
who should be arrested and prosecuted
for selling impure milk and is maliciously
ruining the business of honest men."
Shortly after filing the c6mplaint In the
foregoing action Isaac Leipsic filed With
the Board of Health charges against
Dockery similar in their nature to those
made in the civil action, so far as the
Milk Inspector was concerned, namely, in
competency, intoxication while on duty,
favoritism to some and malicious perse
cution of others. Leipsic asks the privi
lege of appearing before the board with
witnesses to prove the truth of his asser
tions. As Dockery has gone to the Native
Sons' celebration at San Jose his version
of the controversy could not be obtained.
Verdict on the Whitelaw.
The United States Inspectors of Local
and Foreign Steam Vessels have conclud
ed their investigation into the cause of thÂ«
loss of the steamer Whitelaw by fire on
the 4th of March of this year at Skaguay.
and returned a report yesterday to the ef
fect that the fire was caused by sponta
neous combustion of oiled sacks and
clothing among the effects of the passen
gers. The steamer was a total loss. Her
value was $50,000. The value of the cargo
was unknown.

VON ARNOLD'S
LEGAL WIFE
IS DESTITUTE
Sues the Bogus Baron
for Divorce.
ELOPED WITH JEANINE YOUNG
HOW D. M. DELMAS' NEMESIS
WAS CLEVERLY DUPED.
Presiding Superior Judg-e Belcher
Holds That Divorce Is a
Luxury and Should Be
Paid For.
The legal -wife of "Baron yon Turk
helm," a royal name assumed by Charles
J. yon Arnold, when the glitter of gold
caused him to c:ist honor to the winds,
entt-r into a pretended marriage with
Jeanine Young, the nemesis of the elo
quent lawyer D. M. Delmas, and flee to
foreign lands, is In this city toiling for a
livelihood. She no longer wishes that the
name Yon Arnold should make her many
cares the harder to bear, and yesterday
she applied to the Superior Court for a
divorce from the man who dishonored
himself and her and asks that she be al
lowed to resume her maiden name, Emma
Paul.
A decree of divorce is asked for by the
unfortunate woman on three groundsâln
fidelity, failure to provide and willful de
sertion. She asks for no alimony, per
haps knowing that even if the court saw
lit to award her any sum she asked it
would be next to impossible to collect it.
To substantiate the allegation of infidelity
Mrs. yon Arnold refers to the pretended
marriage between her husband and Jean
ine Young. This scandal, the more inter
esting by reason of the connection of the
eminent lawyer, whose life was made a
burden by the bride for months preced
ing the ceremony that was, in fact, a
farce, occurred in April of last year. After
the knot was tied, legally thought Jean
ine, illegally knew Yon Arnold, the couple
went to Montreal and thence to London.
They lived together its man and wife, and
this fact will undoubtedly be accepted by
the court as sufficient proof that the first
contention of the plaintiff is true. That
he deserted his wife there can also be no
question, and her allegation that he has
failed to provjde her with the common ne
cessities of life will be easy to substan
tiate.
George W. Monteith, who represents
Mrs. yon Arnold, Is greatly incensed at
the action of Judge Belcher in refusing to
allow the complaint for divorce to go on
record without prepayment of fees. Mrs.
yon Arnold is all but destitute. She is toil
ing here and there to keep body and soul
together, and the payment of several dol
lars to file the action for divorce was more
than her slender purse would admit. In
consequence her attorney appeared before
Judge Belcher yesterday and asked the
court to make an order remitting the pre
payment of fees in this case, explaining to
the court his client's distressed financial
condition. "Judge Belcher, notwithstand
ing my plea on behalf of this poor woman,
refused to make the order," said Mr. Mon
teith. "He stated that divorce was a lux
ury and that those who wished to enjoy
the luxury should pay for it. I demurred
to the stand taken by the Judge, but when
I found that he could not be moved, I In
formed him that I considered his stand in
the matter contemptible. We had a
stormy meeting and a stormier parting,
the result being that Mrs. yon Arnold had
to go from frfend to friend and borrow
the amount before the paper could be
placed on file. If Justice must be pur
chased, then the very poor are to be pitied
for Justice is not for them, at least on
earth."

BABY TOLAND
WILL HAVE A
BIG NESTEGG
Grandmother's Estate
Settled.
HE GAINS NEARLY $20,000
COMPROMISE EFFECTED AFTER
MANY DAYS.
The Boy's Attorneys Regard the
Agreement as a Restora
tion of His Inherit
ance.
The estate of Mrs. Mary B. Toland,
widow of Dr. H. H. Toland of the
medical college of that name, who died
in November, 1895, at the Occidental
Hotel In this city, has at last been set
tled after a bitter contest in the courts
of nearly three years' duration. By the
terms of the coi.. promise decedent's
grandson, William. Gridley Toland, will
receive nearly $20,000 in lieu of the
pittance bequeathed him under the pro
visions of the two wills left by her.
About ten dÂ»â 8 after Mrs. Toland's
i death Hugo H. Toland, her son, filed a
! document which he alleged to be her
last will and testament, at the same
'. time asking for letters of administra
! tion. In this will, which was rather
' eccentric in its verbiage and provisions,
Mrs. Toland left her grandson $20iW,
but the bulk of her estate, valued at
1 $100,000, she left to her son, Hugo H.
: Toland.
Mrs. Trella Toland, the daughter of
Mrs. Clara Foltz of this city and
mother of William Gridley Toland, at
once engaged Samuel M. Shortridge to
represent the latter's interests. Mr.
Shortridge immediately filed a contest
in behalf of his little client, at that
time but six years of age, claiming that
the will offered for probate as that of
Mrs. Toland was not signed by the tes
tator; that it was not duly witnessed,
and that the testator was not of sound
mind and was therefore incompetent to
make a will.
Contemporaneous with the filing of
the contest Mr. Shortridge demanded a
jury trial and this was granted, but as
the affairs of the estate required im
mediate attention no objection was
made to the issuance of special letters
of administration to Hugo H. Toland.
It must first be understood, however,
that the real objection to the probat
ing of the instrument alleged to be the
will of Mrs. Toland lay in the fact that
the gTandson had been deprived therein
of what his mother and his attorneys
regarded as his rightful inheritance.
They felt that he should have at least
one-half of the entire proceeds of the
estate, and that the paltry $2000 be
queathed him was not sufficient to sat
isfy them in their respective offices of
parent and counsel, charged with the
duty of preserving 1 his rights so long
as he was too young to decide for him
self in such matters.
MR. CHRISTIE FAILS
TO LEAVE HIS ADDRESS
HIS FRIENDS ARE ANXIOUS AS
TO HIS WHEREABOUTS.
It Is Intimated That He Is in Tem
porary Seclusion in the
Country.
The somewhat mysterious and unac
countable disappearance of J. A. Christie
Is causing considerable anxiety and In
quiry among his many friends. Mr. Chris
tie, who is a very popular young man,
had been for over six years the confiden
tial clerk of W. F. Goad, the well-known
lawyer and capitalist, who died a few
weeks ago. He was also secretary of the
Montgomery estate trustees, of whom Mr.
Goad was one, as well as being the attor
ney of the estate. The surviving trustee
of this estate Is A. W. Foster. It had been
rumored that Mr. Christie was short In his
accounts several thousand dollars, but bo
far as the Goad estate Is concerned the
attorney, Richard Bayne, emphatically
states that up to the present he knows of
no such shortage. Mr. Foster has made
a similar declaration regarding thfi ac
counts of the Montgomery estate. Mr.
Christie left the city without giving any
notice whatever to his employers, who are
very much mystified over his singular
conduct. They assert that they cannot In
any way at present account for it.
For eighteen months Mr. Christie had
rooms at the Burnett House. 1364
Market street. On Friday, August
19, he moved from the Burnett,
telling Mr. Hodge, the proprietor, that
he was going to take a room on Sutter
street with a family who recently came
from the East. They were his friends,
and his object was to assist them in a
financial way. Mr. Christie, however, is
evidently not In the city, at least quite a
thorough search by his friends has failed
to locate him In town. Mr. Hodge Bays
that a day or two after Christie's disap
pearance one of his intimate friends called
at the Burnett House and Intimated that
he had simply gone Into the country, but
for what length of time or for what ob
ject he did not say. Among the many
friends who have called at the Burnett to
make Inquiry waa Mr. Foster, who was
there two or three days ago and stated to
Mr. Hodge that Christie's accounts with
the Montgomery estate are correct. An
other friend hinted that there might be a
romance in the case.
Mrs. Hooker, daughter of the late Mr.
Goad, denies that Mr. Christie has run
away, but admits that he is out of sight,
or in other words is in temporary seclu
sion. She asserts that everythingâ what
ever it may beâ will be all right in time.
Mr. Christie stands high in the estma
tlon of all who know him. He is a man of
?'ood habits and moves in good society. So
ar no tangible reason can be given for
his absenting himself from his usual
haunts and place of business without leav
ing his address.

CARTER ESTATE FIGHT IS ON

The Uncle in Turn Has a Spicy Opin
ion of the Character and
Personality of His
Accuser,
The fight for the vast wealth of Thomas
Carter, the millionaire car builder who
died a short time ago, is on. William G.
Black, a nephew of the deceased, filed an
application in the Superior Court yester
day for special letters of administration
upon the estate.
There Is nothing 1 particularly significant
In this fact, but interest is Increased by
I an allegation made by the young man
j who seeks to administer the estate that a
brother of the deceased, Martin Carter,
junior member of the firm of Carter
| Bros., car builders, has a will, left by the
decedent, in his possession, which he re
| fuses to offer for probate. Young Black
states that the deceased left no lineal
i heirs, and that those who are entitled to
the property are two brothers, two sis
ters, a niece and a nephew of the de
ceased, he being the last named, axe al
leges that he is named in the will as one
I of the legatees, and in consequence is
i anxious that the estate be probated and
I distributed to the heirs.
Although frequently requested, he says
that his uncle refuses to offer the will for
probate and refuses to disclose the nature
of the estate and its exact value. This
I being the fact, the petitioner believes that
I the estate is suffering great damage and
I asks to be allowed to administer upon it
that further loss may be prevented.
Martin Carter, who is accused of with
holding the will, is of the opinion that
young Black is hardly capable of ad
ministering the estate and thereby pre
venting its damage, even though it were
suffering for want of a guiding nand. He
was found at his country home at lr
vington last night, and when informed of
his nephew's latest move he was in any
thing but an amiable mood.
"That boy," he declared, "is not re
sponsible for what he says or does. He
has fallen into the hands of lawyers, and
the result will only be to tie up the estate
Indefinitely. Young Black was out here
before his uncle had been buried a week
with lawyers trying to get his sister to
enter into the deal with him, but she re
fused to have anything to do with it. He
is a cripule and has epileptic fits and
spells. He Is absolutely irresponsible for
anything he does. He drinks, and this
makes him worse. While In one of these
spells he has fallen easy prey to any who
see an opportunity to bleed him.
"My brother Thomas made provision for
every one of his relatives and heirs, and
in due time the estate will be distributed,
but this act of my nephew's will not
hurry matters at all. I am now engaged
in the preliminary steps necessary to
make a showing, and as I have thirty
| days I shall not be in any hurry to do so
I till I am ready. Further than this I ao
I not care to discuss the matter, as I am
anxious to avoid all publicity, and i would
much rather that not a word should be
said as coming from me, as I have not
talked before.
William G. Black is the son of a sister
of the Carter brothers, Mrs. George
Black, who lived in Oakland before her
death several years ago. His sister, Miss
Georgia, resides with Martin Carter, and
he did until he became a burden by rea
son of his social habits when ho went to
Oakland. Last Saturday he is said to
have had an epileptic fit at Centerville.
PRECITA VALLEY CLUB.
Wants a Park Completed and tne
Bryant Street Sewer Nuis
ance Abated.
The Precita Valley Improvement Club
Is now forcing with renewed vigor upon
the Board of Supervisors its former de
mands, which have laid dormant for so
long a time.
This is an old club, and has Its regu
lar meetings. Treasurer John T. Gra
ham, who is one of the main workers,
said: "Our principal proposition just now
is to complete Bernal Park, which means
the filling of Bryant street from Twenty
sixth to Army. The Board of Supervis
ors have been asked for an appropriation
of $12,000 to be used for this purpose. It
13 now an assured fact that the money
will be soon forthcoming:, and then we
will have one of the prettiest little places
of recreation In the city. The contem
plated park is to be 800 feet long and 120
feet wide. The city owns the land, and
has paid $6500 for the street work sur
rounding It already.
"Another thing," said Mr. Graham, "Is
the abatement of the Bryant street sewer
nuisance. That sewer now dumps and
gives out Its poisonous germs from
Twenty-sixth to Army streets, endanger
ing the lives of thousands of people. It
could be obviated if It was filled in from
Twenty-sixth to Army streets, but we
cannot have It done. The contract has
been let to John Kelso, but he has delay
ed the work to such an extent that the
matter may have to be taken out of his
hands. This pestilence affects Immedi
ately the children of the Columbian
Grammar School, the St. Peter's School,
under the charge of the Sisters of Mer
cy, where hundreds of children go, and
also the residents in that vicinity.
"This large sewer takes the drainage
from the Twenty-fourth, Twenty-fifth
and Twenty-sixth street sewers. There
fore the nuisance may be easily imag
ined."
FAST SERVICE.
The Resumption of the Santa Fe
Spcial East and West Bound.
The California limited over the Santa
Fe route will be resumed, commencing
November 2. Trains will leave Los Ang
eles eostbound and Chicago westbound
every Monday, Wednesday and Saturday,
running through in two and a half days.
This will be the fastest train ever sched
uled between California and Chicago.
The train will be about the same as last
season, consisting of double drawing
room palace sleeping cars, composite car
and dining car, the latter managed by
Fred Harvey. The train will reach Chi
cago in ample time to make morning
connections for points east, making in all
about two and a half days from Califor
nia points to New York and Boston.
This Week's Wasp.
General Bhafter's conflict with the yel
low-headed hydra Is the subject of the
chief cartoon In this week's Wasp, and
shows in striking fashion the greater
tasks he has before him, even after the
Cuban campaign Is over. Candidate Ma~
quire and his cohorts are shown in pro
cessional array marching to the stale old
war cry that they have howled to no pur
pose for years. Random sketches give in
humorous detail the chief events of the
week that have a comic aspect. There
are splendid half-tone Illustrations of
ships of the British navy, the yeomen of
the guard at the Tower of London, the
bandstand in Golden Gate Park, and other
interesting subjects. Senator Mulrooney
makes some Important disclosures as to
how the Democratic machine is being run
and Tabltha Twiggs discourses on anony
mous letters, divorce and other gossip
An intensely tragic short story, the usual
dramatic criticism and musical notes and
comments complete the table of contents
of the current issue.

BRITISH TRIBUTE
TO COLONEL HAY
Congratulations Alloyed
With Regrets.
AN ANGLO-AMERICAN ADDRESS
BOND OF SYMPATHY BETWEEN
THE NATIONS.
Cemented by the Eminent Tact and
Courtesy of the Retiring Em
bassador â A Happy
Response.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
LONDON, Sept. B.â A committee of
the Anglo-American League, headed by
its chairman, James Brice, presented
the United States Embassador, Colo
nel John Hay, to-day with an address
congratulating him upon his accept
ance of the portfolio of Secretary of
! State at AVashln^ton. The address ex
| pressed regret at his departure from
| this country, "where you have dis
! charged the weighty and delicate dv-
J ties of Embassador with such eminent
I tact, judgment and courtesy as to win
the cordial appreciation and confidence
of the British people."
Continuing, the address says: "It
i has been your fortune to represent your
country here at a time of exceptional
interest, when the war, now happily
ended, gave occasion for an expression
of the feelings of affection and sympa
thy toward the United States, which
the British people have long enter
tained, but never before have they been
so conspicuously manifested. You
carry back the assurance of the depth
and strength of these feelings. The
1 principle that there ought to be perma
nent friendship and cordial co-opera
! tion between the British empire and
I the American republic lÂ» one that all
I parties and all statesmen here agree in
1 regarding as a fundamental principle
| of British foreign policy, and by it the
whole people desire that their govern
ment should be guided.
"We rejoice to believe that in your
country corresponding sentiments are
entertained and that corresponding
; principle is now largely accepted.
\ Knowing that no one holds these con
1 victions more firmly than yourself, or
j can express them in clear and more
felicitous terms, we gladly acknowledge
I the great service you have rendered to
â  both nations, and console ourselves on
; your retirement by the reflection that
i you are called to duties in your own
country even wider in their scope and
graver in their responsibility and more
important In the results they may se
! cure."
Mr. Brice, during the course of the
j remarks which he made upon the oc
i casion, dwelt on the "admiration for
the splendid gifts and boundless en
ergy of the people of the United States
and the sympathy with the principles
of their constitution," which have been
quietly ripening among the British
people and which this year found a
sudden and hearty expression. He
also said he saw in the universality of
i these sentiments here, and the fact that
! they were reciprocated by a large ma
jority of the Americans, a happy au
gury, as he believes that nothing could
contribute more to the peace of the
world and the welfare of both nations
than the sense of essential unity of
the two peoples.
Colonel Hay, in reply, referred to the
gratification he experienced in hearing
such words from men so qualified by
experience and character to speak for
the British with certainty. He added:
"My voice has no such sanction as
yours, but I give it for what it is worth
to assure you that your sentiments of
kinship and amity are reciprocated to
the utmost in my country."
The Embassador also said that his
work in England had been made easy
by the instructions he had received
from home and by the frankness and
fairness of her Majesty's Ministers and
of all Englismen with whom he had
come in contact. He then aid: "On
sides of the ocean the conviction is al
both sides of the ocean the conviction
is almost universal that a clear, cordial
and friendly understanding between
Great Britain and the United States is
a necessity of civilization. I shall hold
myself signally fortunate if I am able
to do anything to continue and
strengthen the relations of fraternal
amity between our nations."
THE EIGHTH REGIMENT.
Soldiers Jubilant Over the Pay
master's Coming This
Morning.
CAMP BARRETT, BATHER TRACT,
Sept. B.â Never since the advent of the
Eighth Regiment at Camp Barrett has
enthusiasm run as high as to-day, when
the welcome news was received from
headquarters that Uncle Sam's paymaster
will visit the camp to-morrow and pay
off.
The men are also jubilant over the fact
that six companies of the Eighth will
break camp next Saturday and go to Fort
Vancouver, while the other campanies
will also be apportioned for garrison duty
at Alcatraa, Benicia and at Fort Point.
There has been considerable talk about
the spread of typhoid fever at Camp Bar
rett and the fact that eight cases have
been recently reported. Regimental Sur
geon and the hospital officers, however,
emphatically deny all such reporte.
"There have been but two coses under
suspicion of typhoid, but nothlngserious,"
said one of the officers to-night. More
over, Colonel Henshaw has been person
ally inspecting the camp and has given
many orders in regard to cleanliness.
BOBBED A KLONDIKEB.
Three Men and Two Women Arrested
on Complaint of Peter Frish.
Charles Erickson, Frank McGill, Ben
Riggans, Aggie Evans and Nellie Brown
were arrested last evening on the sus
picion that they were implicated in the
robbery of Peter Frish, a returned Klon
diker, who claims that he was relieved
of $500. Frish is staying at the Com
mercial Hotel, and Monday night went
out to see the town. About 6 o'clock the
next morning he found himself in the
Gold Field Saloon, 914 Kearny street,
owned by Charles Erickson, and began
to have a good time with the inmates
of the place. He finally became Intoxi
cated, and while in that condition was
knocked down and robbed of $500.
The miner did not report the matter to
the police until yesterday afternoon, when
Detectives Wren and Dlnan and Officers
Cavanaugh, Williams and McMahon were
detailed on the case. Frish took them to
the Gold Field Saloon and pointed out the
parties arrested as the people with whom
he had been drinking and who he thinks
were the ones who robbed him.
Barnes' Property Attached.
An attachment suit was brought yester
day by J. I. Macks against Captain W. S.
Barnes of the Eighth California Volun
teers and present District Attorney of the
City and County of San Francisco, to re
cover a balance of $117 62 claimed on a bill
of household goods sold to the defendant
by W. & J. Sloane & Co., the account be
ing subsequently turned over to Macks,
ANNEXATION OR
INDEPENDENCE
Question Now Discussed
in Havana.
FAVOR UNCLE SAM'S AID
REPUBLIC UNDEB AMERICAN
PROTECTION POPULAR.
Union Expected Between the Rad
ical Autonomists and the
Leaders of the
Insurgents.
Special cable to The Call and the New York
Herald. Copyrighted, 1898, by James Gor
don Bennett.
HAVANA, Sept. B.â Annexation or
independence. These are the words that
Havana is conjuring with to-day. A
long wait for the arrival of the Ameri
can Commission had an effect on the
nerves of the populace already keyed
to a breaking point by three months of
blockade hunger.
A vast amount of political discussion
is being done. At first the composite
opinion of all men of property or posi
tion, or even of respectability, was for
annexation. During the last week the
voice of the ultra Cuban party, the ir
reconcilables, is for independence or
nothing.
A union has been effected between
what is known as the radical autono
mists and insurgent leaders. A plat
form has been arranged and a definite
plan of action been agreed upon, urg
ing the claims for absolute independ
ence upon the American Commission
ers. The leaders of the radical auton
omist party are Eduardo Dolz and
Elizo Giberga. Dolz is a member of
Blanco's autonomist Cabinet. Up to
the time of the signing of the protocol
he was loud in protests of loyalty to
Spain. Giberga is a Havana lawyer,
for years prominent In autonomist
councils. These two men are in con
stant communication with the insur
gents, and personally visited during the
last few days nearly all the insurgent
leaders in Havana province. Speaking
of the new party Mr. Dolz said to me:
"The principles of our new movement
for independence are but little under
stood. We believe in independence â
for the good of Cuba, mark you â not
for the good of the insurgents or auton
omists nor any particular class or
party, but the entire population of the
island, without regard to their previous
political opinions. We have the same
mistrust of the insurgents' disorderly
methods that the annexation party has,
but declare that here in Havana and
other cities of Cuba are men capable of
governing, who understand Cuba's na
tional character and needs far better
than any Americans do. We do not say
absolute independence will be an im
mediate success. We must have a pro
tectorate by the United States, but let
the protectorate be withdrawn as soon
as possible."
It Is still far less difficult to obtain
interviews in Havana favoring annex
ation than statements in support of Cu
ban independence. As far as can be
estimated, also, the supporters of an
nexation are a more numerous body In
the city and certainly among those of
the highest standing, representing
property and commercial interests.
DISCUSSING THE TAX LEVY.
Much Planning Necessary to Keep
Within Legal Limits.
OAKLAND, Sept. B.â The City Council,
sitting as a committee of the whole, pro
ceeded this evening with the work of fix
ing the tax levy. It was decided to al
low enough money to place two free read
ing rooms in the annexed district. TTTe
estimates for the School Department, ag
gregating 1302,479, will be taken up next
Monday evening when the Board of Edu
cation will be present.
An animated discussion took place over
the matter of placing $80,000 in the tax
levy for the dredging of Lake Merritt,
and Auditor Snow stated that there
are many problems yet to be solved before
the legal aspect of the matter is clear.
Mr. Snow had cut the amount to $42,000
on the ground that the amount had better
be placed in the levy of two fiscal years.
Messrs. Girard and Cuvelller were of the
same opinion, and Woodward advised
them to stand by their colors, which caused
a laugh. Woodward said the next Coun
cil might complete the work, and Girard
said there need be no worry, as no doubt
Woodward could be elected unanimously,
and this created much amusement.
Auditor Snow made a speech that sug
gested many difficulties in the way of
making all the improvements contemplat
ed. He said that under the law the money
obtained for parks and boulevards could
not be used for any other purpose, not
even building bulkheads. He also hinted
that there were 16,000 tax payers who
would not be directly benefited by the im
provement and who would not pass by
any chance to block It. Further, there is
the annexed district to be considered, as
the legality of the matter is still before
the Supreme Court. Mr. Snow's speech
was somewhat of a damper. As the tax
levy, independent of the Lake matter,
cannot legally exceed $1 27 there will have
to be much pruning yet done in the de
partments, as the estimates at present
call for about $1 50.
Persistent Socialists.
OAKLAND, Sept. B.â Another street
speaker, Thomas Bersford, was arrested
to-night on Broadway, and put up $20
ball. This makes the fourth socialist ar
rested during the last four nights, and
the leaders say there are twenty-five more
men and women that will be arrested at
the rate of one a night. Each will de
mand a jury trial, and they declare they
will carry the matter to the Supreme
Court to test the ordinance prohibiting
street Bpeaking within the fire limits. In
the Police Court to-day E. J. Grebs plead
ed not guilty and his case was set for
September 23, the day after the Wilklns
trial. The case of James Andrews was
set for the 27th.
Captain Carson in Danger.
OAKLAND, Sept. B.â Captain Frank
Carson, of the Police Department, who
has been sick for some time with appen
dicitis, was worse to-day, and this after
noon underwent an operation. He pos
sesses a vigorous constitution, and this.
It is believed, may carry him through. At
a late hour to-night the patient was slum
bering.

Irving M.Scott May B<
the People's Choice.
AS A NOMINEE FOR MAYO
A GENERAL DEMAND FOR HIM
TO LEAD THE PARTY.
Maguire Succeeds in Again Making
Himself Unpopular With the
German Democrats of
the City.
Although the date of the Republican
municipal convention has not yet been
fixed much speculation Is being In
dulged in regarding the choice of the
party for Mayor. There are many
available candidates among the solid
men of the community, but with 6O
many to choose from there is much
uncertainty and diversity of opinion as
to the particular man upon whom the
choice should fall.
It is this uncertainty that has led
many of the leading Republicans of the
city to express a wish that individual
preferences be set aside and that all
unite in asking Irving M. Scott to ac
cept the nomination. Mr. Scott arrived
in New York a few days ago after an
extended visit to Continental Europe
In behalf of the industrial and commer
cial interests of the Pacific Coast. He
Is due to arrive in San Francisco with
in a few days, and it is more than
probable that upon his arrival here he j
will be approached on the subject that |
Is now uppermost in the minds of those
interested in local municipal affairs.
It is pointed out by the Republican j
leaders that Mr. Scott is perhaps the !
most popular man in California to-day i
bat bis popularity extends to peo- j
pie of all classes and conditions. If he
can be induced to accept the nomina
tion there is no doubt but that he j
would be elected by a majority exceed- j
ing that ever given a candidate of any
party In this city. Having the confi
dence of the people it is certain that he
could and wou.d bring order out of the
chaos that has characterized the con
duct of municipal affairs in San Fran
cisco for so many years.
old Mr. Scott express a reluctance
to enter into public life and thus neg
lect his groat private interests it will
be urged that the term is a shurt one :
an d ; good citizen he owes it
to himself and the community at large
to surrender personal Interests and de-
Mmself to a cause that vitally ef
fects every man, woman and child in j
ban Francisco.
-Much dissatisfaction is being mani
fested on the Democratic side concern
ins the conduct of the affairs of the
party in bjoth the city and State. Now
that v ha.s become apparent to the
dullest among them that Lhe affairs of
vnÂ« pa.it y have been given into the
ing uf the members of the Clan
there can be no doubt as to
â¢what the result will be. In the im
probable event of Magulre's election all
of the fat appointments in the State
will be parceled out to the members of
the clan and the real workers In the
party will be left to a discussion of the
husks. In Ran Francisco the result of
a Democratic victory would be the
same. The Gallaghers, the Manns, tfie
Treacys, the Leggetts and the elect of
the band of single-taxers will secure
the cream of the patronage to the total
exclusion of the party workers. Even
under present conditions no less than a
scnre of the members of the clan are
feeding at the public crib. If the Ma
gruire-Gallagher-Phelan combination
â Is in gaining control of local af
lairs the number will be trebled.
Jud^e Maguire made an attempt yes
terday to "square" himself with the
an Democrats of the city by ap
pointing Oscar Hocks, a member of the
German Democratic Central Club, to
a place on the Democratic State Cen
tral Committee. When Magulre ap
pointed the twenty-one members at
large of the State committee he ignored
the request of the German Club for
rocornitlon. He then added insult to
injury by appointing Dr. Max Magnus,
a man who Is anything but popular
with the memh-rs of the German Club.
"vVh^n this action became known Ma
prulre was informed that he must re
pair his mistake or suffer the loss of
the support of the German Democrats
of the city. This he promised to do.
In the meantime, however, he appointed
Dr. Maernup a member of the purity
committee (if the State committee. Now
that he has appointed Mr. Hocks as a
member of the State committee he finds
that the act has not in any way bet
tered his condition so far as the Ger
man Democrats are concerned.

for Lieutenant Governor, was in the

tho old line Democrats. In this re
ppect he divides the honors with J.

POPS BATTLING
FOR THE NAME
The Big Fight to Open
Saturday.
SECRETARY BROWN TO DECIDE
- - - ; â 
â â _â
SHAN AHAN WILL MAKE A VIG
. OROUS CAMPAIGN.
The Voters in the Outraged Party
Are Combining ,to Defeat
the Fusion
Ticket,
A big legal battle between the regular
Populists and the fusionists for the party
designation on the official ballot will be
formally opened at Sacramento to-mor
row.
The initial decision In the matter must
be given by Secretary of State Brown,
with whom all State tickets must be filed
within a specified time. To-day is the
sixtieth and last day prescribed by law,
but it being a legal holiday. Secretary
Brown has notified the representatives of
the opposing factions of the Populist
party that he will receive their tickets
I and briefs and hear their arguments on
I Saturday at 10 a. m. Thomas V. Cator
will appear for the middle-of-the-road-
Bters, and he is preparing a brief which
; will make a very strong showing for the
I genuine party which was bo ignominlously
sold out to the Democracy.
The Populist State Central Committee
is more than confident that Secretary
Brown will decide in their favor, but if
Ihe does not the fight will be at once
I taken to the Supreme Court. The fuslon
i lsts will doubtless do the same thing if
they are decided against, so a light to the
finish Is assured.
Shanahan and the State Central Com
mittee are waiting for this decision before
opening the campaign. Should they se
cure the recognition they claim to be en
i titled to the Populist State campaign will
j be opened at once in the most vigorous
I style. Shanahan promises to make a lively
gubernatorial fight, and the great ma
jority of the party which remains loyal
to its principles and which repudiates fu
i sion as the fruit of a base betrayal will
rally in a fashion that will be uncom
fortable for the Pinto Populists and their
friends.
The decision as to whom the name
"People's party" belongs will have very
important results. If the regular Popu
liats ultimately lose, the Shanahan ticket
and the campaign will likely be aban
doned, for the candidates could run only
as Independents after filing a petition re
quiring 10,000 names.
If the fusion wing loses the Populist
candidates on the fusion ticket will have
to go on the ballot as simply Democrats.
Even the Silver Republican candidates on
the hybrid ticket, McCraney and Van
Dyke, would appear as pure Democrats
and nothing more.
The general and to-be-expected revolt of
the great majority of the Populists
against the fusion arrangement is show
ing itself daily in Increasing strength. The
great body of the party is filled with a
spirit of independence and loyalty. It
snows itself in the voluminous correspond
ence received from all over the State by
<arleton H. Johnson, secretary of the
State Central Committee, which has its
headquarters in the Parrott building, and
also in the way that county committees
are reorganizing.
"The Populists generally throughout the
State feel that they have been sold out
and they are knifing fusion right and
left." said Secretary Johnson yesterday.
"We expect now that Shanahan will poll
as large a vote as Webster did four years
ago. To show how repugnant fusion is to
the mass of the party, two years ago,
even with all the whoop for Bryan, 20.000
Populists voted for the Populist electors,
when there was a doubt that the vote
would be totalized an<l the State Central
Committee urged all Populists to vote for
the Democratic electors. You remember
that the same fusion electors appeared
twice on the ballotâ once as Democrats
and once as Populists.
"The county committees are being rap-
I idly reorganized and there will soon be
j straight People's party organizations in
! all the counties. In Shasta, Tuolumne,
i Tulare and other counties straight county
I tickets have already been nominated. In
j Kings County it was agreed to support a
county fusion ticket, but all Populists are
to support the Shanahan ticket. Lines
are being closely drawn and no Populist
will be supported for a local office who
! will not support the straight State ticket.
i Populist local fusion candidates thus do
not dare to support Maguire, even if so
Inclined. The members of the party feel
outraged and they are bound to beat fu
son. No county convention which rati
fies fusion is recognized by the State
! Central Committee-."
A howl of indignation is being raised
i throughout the State by a circular which
has been sent out by George D. Gillespie,
secretary of the State Central Committee
of the fusion Pops. It is sent to State Cen
tral Committeemen and levies a tax of 50
cents "upon each candidate who comes be
; fore the several county conventions of the
People's party desiring nomination for
j public office, whether the candidate is
! successful before the convention or not."
j This applies to Democrats eeekinp fusion
I nominations as well. It is explained that
i expensive legal battles are in siKht and the
four-bit pieces are wanted to fight "those
who are attempting to Bteal the party
name."
North End Republican Club.
The Republicans of the north end of
the Forty-first Assembly District met last
night and organized the North End Re
publican Club. E. M. Galvin was elected
president and L. J. Hart secretary. It
was decided to hold a mass-meeting In the
district at Plxley Hall in two weeks. Hon.
Z. T. Dodge was unanimously Indorsed
for State Senator from the Twenty-second
District, and J. L. Waller for Supervisor
of the Second Ward.
Republicans Are Active.
The Republican voters of Precinct No.
3 of the Thirty-fourth Assembly District
met last evening at Progress Hall and or
ganized a permanent club. Seventy mem
bers signed the roll. The officers elected
were Charles J. Klass. president, and W
J. Winterbottom, secretary. The club will
meet again at the same place on the
evening of September 21.
The Bear Club.
The Bear Club held a meeting last even
ing and indorsed ..he nomination of Julius
Kahn for Congress. A. B. Truman, Ed J.
Casey, A. W. Voorsanger, George O'Neill
J. T. Hayes, John Woods and others made
remarks for Republican success.
"Lucrezia Borgia" at the Tivoli.
The second opera of this noteworthy
week at the Tivoli was "Lucrezia Borgia,"
composed by Donizetti. This venerable
opus has a very strong libretto, and its
music is well adapted to inspire the sing
ers to their best capabilities. Alfonso,
Duke of Ferrara, was sung by the effer
vescent artist, Willjam Pruette. with his
usual dash and spirit, and could not have
been in better hands. His strong bary
tone is ideally suited to these heavy
parts, where manliness and knightly gal
lantry Is Invaluable. Frl. Marie Brandts
as Lucrezia was very good, if one makes
allowance of her German schooling, which
seems out of place at times with a cast
of other artists. Her voice, however, is
strong and pleasing and under control at
all times. Zerni's Gennaro deserves great
credit, as his masterful rendition of one of
the most difficult parts in Italian opera
showed that there are great possibilities
in store for this young artist. Mr. Schus
ter as Gubetta was a refined, well-studied
performance, and Miss Elvla Crox as
Mafflo impressed the audience to due ap
preciation.

Manila.

COLD WATER
POLITICIANS
Alameda Prohibitionists
Will Nominate.
AN INDIGNANT REPUBLICAN
DEMOCRATS ACROSS THE BAT
ARE WAKING UP,
If Fusion Is Effected There Will Be
Ho Lack of Office Seekers,
Political Notes in
General,
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, Sept. 8.
The Prohibitionists will hold their ooun
ty convention to-morrow at the rooms of
the Women's Christian Temperance
Union in the Playter block. They will
nominate a full county ticket, if sufflcient
candidates are available. The ladies will
assist the delegates to enjoy a basket
lunch at noon. On account of the light
vote polled by the party at the last elec
tion it will be necessary for the candi
dates to get their names on thÂ» ballot
by petition.
Weston Trusdale, editor of thÂ« San
Leandro Standard, is in a peck of trou
ble over a misunderstanding concerning
a word which he used in one of bis edi
torials of recent date.
"I have incurred the enmity of a dis
tinguished local Republican," says Trus
dale, "because I stated last week that
during the past few years the San Le
andro Republicans had made many pros
elytes. This learned gentleman hastened
to Inform me with much indignation that
the Republican party is one of high mor
ality, and that it never made a proselyte
in its life. Moreover, he wants the
Standard to apologize for 'casting a dirty
slur on the Republicans of San Leandro.'
I don't know what my indignant friend
thinks the word 'proselyte' means, but
I suppose he thinks it signifies something
very bad. Anyhow he is welcome to any
apology he may see fit to require."
The Democrats are now beginning to
wake up, and are looking for county can
didates. Justice St. Sure of Alameda
would like to be District Attorney, and
Edward A. Holman, N. J. Manons and
R. B. Myers are also spoken of in case
fusion is agreed upon. Charles E. Lloyd,
J. C. McLeod and R. M. McKillican are
mentioned for Sheriff. County Treasurer
Sanford will try to succeed himself.
Thomas Moran of the Fourth Ward may
be a candidate for Supervisor, and J. M.
Shay may run for the Assembly In the
Forty-ninth District. Seth Mann is a can
didate for State Senator from his dis
trict. Colonel J. J. Lyon is now in town,
and he will probably be a candidate for
some office.
The Democratic County Central Com
mittee will meet on Saturday evening to
fix the date of the primaries and the
county convention. The Populist Coun
ty Central Committee has called the pri
maries for September 20, but has not yet
fixed a date for the county convention,
which is to be determined by the chair.
It is now believed to be probable that
the convention of the Populists will be
on the same day as the Democratic gath
ering, and that a fusion plan of naming
the candidates will be agreed upon.
SUTRO'S ESTATE
WELL CARED FOR
THE ADMINISTRATORS ENTER
UPON THEIR DUTIES.
Ask the Court for Permission to Ex
pend the Entire Income for the
Maintenance of the
Property.
Mrs. Dr. Emma Merritt and W. R. 11.
Adamson, who last week were appointed
special administrators of the estate of
the late Adolph Sutro, have entered upon
their duties with a vim. Yesterday they
filed a petition in the Superior Court for
permission to expend money toward the
maintenance and improvement of the es
tate. The petitioners state that the
monthly expense of the baths, the Cliff
House, Sutro Heights and of the valu
able library left by the deceased philan
thropist amounts to about $5000. This
amount, they state, is about equal to the
present income of the estate, and they ask
to be allowed to expend the entire in
come for the purposes set forth. Besides
the Immediate expenses of the estate the
petitioners state that In the near future
an assessment of $2 a share on the stock
of the Sutro Railroad Company, held by
the estate, must be met, which will de
duct no immaterial sum from the assets
In the possession of the executors. It is
not thought that the heirs to the estate
will make any objection to the petition, as
a thorough care of the property will en
hance its value, and when the day for dis
tribution comes all will reap the 'benefit.
CLOSE OF THE IRISH FAIR.
Last Days of the Big Exposition of
the Celtic Union.
The Irish Fair Is drawing near Its close,
there being but two days more of the big
exposition.
Last night was given over to the Im
proved Order of Hed Men. Several tribes
were present in the regalia of their order.
Admission day will be duly celebrated at
the fair to-day.
The women who have done so much for
the success of the fair will be entertained
at a grand ball on Tuesday evening next.

PHELAN IS
RENOMINATED
FOR MAYOR
Unanimous Choice of
the Non-Partisans.
POINTED QUESTIONS ASKED
A PLATFORM WAS PRESENTED
BUT NOT ADOPTED.
It Was Announced That Phelan,
Like Maguire, Will Stand
on Any Kind of a
Platform.
James D. Phelan was renominated for
Mayor last night by the Citizens' Non-
Partisan Convention. The delegates were
so anxious to carry out that part of their
work that they could not wait until a
platform had been adopted. It was an
nounced on behalf of Phelan, however,
that he would subscribe to any platform
adopted by the convention. The Mayor
has evidently taken lessons from James
Gallagher Maguire, the Populist-Silver
Republican-Democratic nominee for Gov
ernor, who has swallowed three plat
forms.
The convention was called to order by
George K. Fitch shortly after 8 o'clock,
and a call of the roll by Secretary Isaac
T. Milliken showed that a Quorum was
present.
The first order of business waa the re
ceiving of the report of the committee on
platform. Frank J. Sullivan, a brother
in-law of Mayor Phelan, announced that
the committee was ready to report. He
then proceeded to read tne report, which
is published In these columns in full.
When Sullivan concluded reading the
platform prepared. George L. Center
claimed the attention of Chairman Fitch.
He said that he thought section 16, relat
ing to the purchase of lands by the city,
was not couched in terms strong enough
to show the feeling of the people in re
gard to the looting of the public treasury.
"It Is time that these raids on the treas
ury, made under the guise of municipal
improvement, should be stopped," said he,
"and we should pledge our candidates to
light against these raids. Take, for in
stance, the proposed purchase of land for
a zoo. Every one knows what it means."
"That matter was considered by the
committee," replied A. \V. Halliday, "and
the conclusion arrived at was that the
present Board of Supervisors would not be
able to carry through the project."
"By the way," inquired Delegate "Web
ster, "why is it that Mayor Phelan is the
only official commended? Why not name
the honest members of the Board of Su
pervisors and the honest members of the
Board of Education?"
"We have only one Mayor," replied
Halliday. "and consequently no invidious
distinctions can be made.
"I would like to know, Mr. Chairman,"
persisted Webster, "if it is a hard Job to
put one's finger on the honest men 'v>n
both boards?"
E. J. Le Breton put a stop to further
discussion on that line by moving that
the matter of adopting the platform be
laid over for one week. The motion pre
vailed with a suggestion that any mem
bers having alterations or amendments to
offer present them in the meantime to the
committee on platform for consideration.
The report of the committee on order
of business and rules was adopted with
out discussion.
The question as to whether the candi
dates for Supervisors could be selected
from any part of the city or whether
they must be taken from the ward In
which they live, was referred to the Judi
ciary comittee.
The committee on municipal nomina
tions reported progress. Chairman Lie
Breton said the names of various aspi
rants had been canvassed, but that no
conclusion had been arrived at other than
one. the office of Mayor. "The commit
tee," said he in conclusion, "decided to
unanimously recommend James D. Phe
lan for renomination."
On motion of L. Altschul It was decided
to increase the county committee from
twenty-five to forty members. It was
further decided that any member who ab
sented himself from three consecutive
sessions of the convention without satis
factory excuse would be considered as
having resigned.
Delegate Perkins then moved that .the
rules be suspended and that the conven
tion proceed to nominate a candidate for
Mayor. The motion was carried, and Le
Breton placed Phelan in nomination. He
said that Mayor Phelan had carried out
all of the promises he made to the Non-
Partisan convention two years ago, and
that he thought it would only be a grace
ful compliment to nominate him before
any of the other conventions met. "We
desire to see him again in the chair,"
said Le Breton, "and we further desire
that any higher aspirations he may have
will not prevent him being the first May
or of San Francisco under the new char
ter."
There were no other nominations, and
the secretary was instructed to cast the
ballot for Phelan. When his nomination
was officially announced the delegates
rose and gave three cheers for Phelan.
The convention then adjourned to meet
next Thursday night after instructing
Secretary Milliken to telegraph to Mayor
Phelan officially notifying him of his
nomination.
The question asked by Delegate Web
ster why the honest members of the
Board of Supervisors were not mentioned
in the platform was not relished by Phe
lan's friends. It is a matter of political
history that Phelan refused to head the
Democratic municipal ticket two years
ago unless he was allowed to name the
Supervisors. He named them, and every
time the "Solid Nine" is mentioned Phe
lan has the shivers, for he is the political
foster father of the majority of them.
NON-PARTISAN PLATFORM.
The Non-Partisan party, composed of men
of various political parties, exists solely to
protect the best Interests of the city of.
Ban Francisco and Its. citizens.
It proclaims Its absolute freedom from
political bias and Its non-interference in
national or State politics, except when nec
essary to protect municipal interests.
Called Into existence by i the disgraceful
condition of affairs that has developed since
the dissolution of : the old People's party
arid the wanton disregard for the rights and
Interests of the â  citizens of; San Francisco;
in the management of municipal affairs the
Non-Partisan party proposes to influence the
coming municipal election in the Interest
of good' and honest government. , ,- % â 
"With the approval of the new charter by
the vote of the , people the prospect of a
better government baaed on . business prin- 9
ciples and . pledged -to economy, and effi
ciency encourages the â  Non-Partisan party
in the belief that the citizens of San Fran-.
Cisco desire good government and . will sup
port its nominees in | the coming election.
The Non-I'artiean party recognizes that
In the government of a city ' party politics
has no place, and that â¢ the â¢ hordes of hun
gry followers of - political : bosses - must -be
driven from the City Hall, which they, have
polluted too long with â  their dishonest prac
: tices, their bribery v and corruption. .- The
management "of â  city affairs must -be taken '
from = their inefficient < and wasteful â  hands .
and given to honest and competent officers.
We believe the time has arrived when the
people of San Francisco will no longer sub
mit to be ruled and plundered by dishonest :
: and ':â  political . bosses. The Non-Partlsan
party Â» therefore â  announces â  the â¢ following '
principles, : which â  shall guide Its * nominees, â¢
and declares ; the i same * to be ; the platform
on which they must stand: I ; r v â .*.
â¢ 1. This convention will . nominate - for of
fice,' irrespective of party or. party nomina
tions, such men as it believes to be honest,
capable and fit for the office for which they
are nominated, and .will not nominate or in
dorse any candidate for the Legislature who
has not agreed in writing to vote for the
ratification; of the new charter. -
-: 2. The - convention ;â¢; condemns .. any effort :
to defeat the nomination or election of men
because eof '. religious belief. â¢- It ' believes -In ?
freedom ) of I speech i and I affirms 4ts | loyalty
to . those wise j provisions of the I Federal and I
State 'â ' constitutions : - which *â " guarantee, to
every citizen I the | fullest I religious ! liberty. v'
;K 8. The ; public - schools â¢we t consider ; espe- 1
..oUUk Uie flttlcKUÃrd ot our public lastltu
tions and personal liberties. The effi
ciency of the school should be Increased,
Its Instruction simplified, industrial educa
tion should be foetered, the sanitary con
dition of the schooihouses and ths phyÃi
cal development of the pupils should re
ceive the attention and care of the teach
ers. In order to Insure the best work we
insist on the prompt payment of the salar
ies of the teachers and other employes of
the School Department. The appropriation
made last year was adequate to the needs
of the department economically adminis
tered. "With shameless disregard for the
interests of the public and the children
much of this money was wasted in Jobbery.
Teachers must be paid by law, Irrespective
of the appropriation made. The present
board has diverted the money set aside for
their Balaries and forced a deficit, which
must be made up in this fiscal year. This
act is a fraud upon the public and merits
the severest condemnation.
4. We demand that the same rules which
govern a well-conducted business house
shall apply to the government of the city
of San Francisco, and we, therefore,
pledge our nominees to an economical ana
Judicious administration of our municipal
affairs and limit them to an expenditure
not exceeding one dollar on the one hun
dred dollars of assessable property, within
a limit of 13,500,000, for all municipal pur
poses.
6. Renewed efforts should be made to re
move the Police and Fire Departments
from politics. We believe that the effi
ciency of both these departments ehould be
increased. We require that it shall be
the duty of every patrolman to enforce
the ordinances of the city and to note and
report to the Chief of Police all violations
of the same.
6. In all public work home labor shall
be employed and the productions of the
Btate shall In all cases be given the pref
erence. Eight hours shall be the limit for
a day's work.
7. We require that our nominees shall do
all In their power to secure the confirma
tion by the Legislature of the new charter
and when the charter becomes a law we
require the spirit and the provisions of
the same shall be carried out ' in good
faith.
8. Telephone service has become a neces
sity to our people and the privileges granted
by the city to the telephone companies have
been very liberal and are of great value.
We demand free service to the city and a
reduction In rates to our citizens.
9. Street railroad franchises have been
granted without regard to the convenience
of the public or the value of the franchise.
We require that all unused rails and tracks
shall be removed from the streets as well
as dangerous obstacles, such as the turn
table at Oeary and Kearny, at Post and
Montgomery and the curve at the toot of
Sacramento street.
10. The charge for gas and electric light
should be reduced and the quality improved
and the city, subject as it Is to foggy and.
dark nights, should have Its lamps lighted
every night in the year.
11. The acquisition by the city of publlo
utilities is very desirable and we favor such
acquisition whenever practical. Especially
do we recognize the importance of this city
owning its gas and electric lighting plant
and a system of water works. The rate
for hydrants should not exceed $2 60 each
per annum and 6 per cent dividend on tha
water company's stock should be nut Helen t,
and we pledge our nominees for Supervisors
to reduce rates to bring about this result.
12. We denounce the lamentable failure
and the transparent imbecility of our Su
pervisors In connection with the building
of the Hall of Justice and point this out
as a glaring example of the inefficiency
of the present city government, calling for
a remedy at the hands of the people.
13. Pure food Is essential to the health
of the people. We heartily commend the
efforts now being made to assure the purity
of food and to punish those who adulterate
or sell adulterated food.
14. The Public Pound as now conducted
is a disgrace to the city. We favor the con
trol of the Public Pound by the Society for
the Prevention of Cruelty to Animals, whose
management for four years was satisfactory
to the citizens and In the Interest of de-
cency.
15. The administration of municipal af
fairs during the past two years has been a
deplorable failure. We denounce the pledge
breakers of the Board of Supervisors and
the Board of Education, and condemn their
disregard of the sacred rights of the citi
zens who elected them to office.
In strong contrast to the action of the
above officials, we commend the able and
efficient administration of Mayor Phelan In
the fearless performance of his official duty
at all timeÃ, and also those members of the
Boards of Supervisors and of Education who
have labored faithfully to serve the peo
ple.
16. We are opposed to the further pur
chase of property or the construction of
buildings by the present clt- authorities,
and we require that all such projects be
postponed until after action Is taken by the
Incoming Legislature on the new city char
ter, which provides business methods for
such purposes.
17. We require that the expenses of the
County Clerk's office be kept within a limit
of $6000 per month, and we condemn the In
cumbent as a pledge-breaker for the ex
travagance displayed In that office during
the past two years.
18. Real estate and Improvements and In
dustrial entprprlses and the homes of the
people are now taxed at their full value,
whereas, the large quasi-public and other
corporations escape with an insignificant
valuation. The Supreme Court In the case
of the Ran Jose Gas Company vs. January,
has laid down the rule by which corporate
property may be assessed at approximately
Its market or real value. We therefore
pledge our nominees for Assessor, and for
the Board of Supervisors, when sitting aa a
Board of Equalization, to follow the de
cision aforesaid.
19. The Grand Jury system has often
proved to be a shield for crime. The same
men are reappolnted term after term. We
therefore pledge our nomlneess for the Su
perior Court to select proper material for
the Grand Jury, and In no case to ap
point a citizen who has served In that
capacity at any time during the last three
preceding years.
20. The office of District Attorney Is the
most lmnortnnt of all the offices In the
municipal government. He has power hy
filing an Information to hold for trial any
boss or officeholder who Is charged with
bribery and corruption, and he can also sup
press many evils which are a disgrace to
our city. With a fearless, independent Dis
trict Attorney in office we believe that
School Directors. Supervisors and other
officials would abstain from public rob
beries with which their names are coupled.
The heads of great corporations who deal
with them would refrain from tempting
the weak and easily corrupted. A new
era would dawn on the city of San Fran
cisco.
21. We earnestly entreat all citizens to
vote against tho Infamous constitutional
amendment known aR number one, being
Senate Amendment No. 41. This would
subject the city of San Francisco to claims
for street work of all kinds done in the past.
It might cause the bankruptcy of the city.
22. To carry out the provisions of the
charter In good faith we pledge our muni
cipal candidates not to contest the seats
of their successors who may be elected
under the charter In November. 1899. for the
reason that the terms for which they are
elected at the forthcoming election are
curtailed by the ratification of the charter
by the Legislature.
In conclusion, the Citizens' Non-Partisan
Convention, in the hope that municipal
affairs cf the city shall be thoroughly re
formed, nnd that Its citizens shall not be
made the mere servants of the unprinci
pled bosses of the great political parties
or grasping corporations, and in the belief
that honesty and integrity should be the
rule and not the exception among city
officials, asks the people of San Francisco
for its hearty and generous support.
A. S. HALLIDIE. Chairman.
FRANK J. SULLIVAN.
PHILIP L. GALPIN.
JOHN E. QUINN,
WILLIAM M. ABBOTT.
WAITING FOR GALLAGHER.
The Mission and Potrero Club Anx
ious About a Franchise.
The Mission and Potrero-avenue Im
provement Club is just now anxously
awaiting to hear from City and County
Attorney Gallagher with reference to the
franchise of the Southern Pacific Rail
road, granting it the privilege of putting
down double tracks across the streets of
the city from the San Mateo County line
to the terminus of the coast division at
Third and Townsend streets. The club
has discovered that the railroad really
has no franchise to keep its present single
track down where it is. and has therefore
submitted the matter to the City Attor
ney for an opinion.
As the request has been In the latter's
possession for about six weeks it begins
to look as if the matter was to be put
over until after election.
If the opinion Is for the railroad it will
hurt Mfiguire. Gallagher's candidate, with
the people. If it is for the people it will
hurt Maguire with the railroad. In either
case it would be injurious to Mr. Maguire,
It is declared, by the club members.
If not answered by Monday a committee
will wait upon City and County Attorney
Gallagher.

The Notorious Dora Fuhrig Arrested

Fuhrlg of having performed a criminal

ing caused the death of a woman in

VALLEY ROAD MAY
USE CHINA BASIN
HARBOR COMMISSIONERS SET
TLE THE DISPUTE.
The Basin Will Be the Place for
the Erection of Freight
Wharves.
The Board of Harbor Commissioners
has at last decided that it cannot with
hold from the Valley Railroad the use of
China Basin as a place for the erection of
freight wharves and buildings for the
handling: of the freight traffic of the road
when the connections are made with this
city. It was so decided at the meeting of
the Board of Harbor Commissioners yes
terday afternoon.
It will be remembered that when the
Valley Railroad began to build its road
from Stockton to Visalia the Legislature
was petitioned for the use of the China
Basin by the new company for freight
traffic purposes. The Legislature then em
powered the Harbor Commissioners to al
low or to refuse it as they saw fit. The
basin was then leased to the Valley Road
by the Harbor Commissioners, but subse
quently the board repudiated its action
and refused to accept the rental tendered
by the Valley Railroad. This dispute hung
fire for some time.

CANNED FRUITS AND SALMON.
The recent arrivals of California fruits have |
all been sold, and resales have been made of
many parcels, Rays the London Grocers' Ga
t*tte. It is generally thought high prices for I
Callfornlan fruits will increase the consump
tion of pines, nnd considering the low price
of these an active demand is expected. The
market for salmon has beer, in a very ani
mated and excited condition, owing to reports
that thp pack on the Fraser River, which has
Just been completed, will show a shortagp of
about 650,000 ca*es as compared with the quan
tity put up last year. Prices have been In
consequence advanced Is to Is 6d per case, and
at the close holders are firm, and the tendency
of prices Is upward, in fact packers have in
many cases cabled their agents not to sell for
forward delivery.

NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NTTW YORK, Bept. Bâ St. Paul made the
market this morning and broke this morning.
Trading â was not on a large scale during the
flay and all Interest centered In the meeting
of the St. Paul directors to act on the divi
dend. The rumor mongers fixed the dividend
rate on the common all the way from 2% to
Shi per cent. Those who paid that It would
be 2H found little credence and the continued
firmness of the stock In the /ace of the recent
heavy tone of the market and the confident
and heavy buying of the early hours of to-day
eeemed to Justify this Incredulity. There was
large buying of Pt. Paul here for London ac
oount and there was very active trading upon
the London curb after the close there, accord-
Ing to cahled reports. The price was rushed
up to 115 without any attention being
given to warning statements that very power
ful Interests In the directorate were opposed
to anything more than the 2Â»* per cent rate.
The eager bolls pointed thai the recent In
crease In the Burlington dividend was kept
â¢nMirouded In the same mystery up to the
moment of Its declaration, when the stock led
a violent upward movement. The showing of
the company for the last fiscal year was cited
â¢s confirming the confidence in an increased
dividend disbursement, the earnings applica
ble to the common stock having amounted to
8 3-10 per cent after extraordinarily heavy ex
penditures for Improvements charged to oper
ating expenses. When the announcement came
about 2:30 p. m., that the directors had made
the second annual dividend only 2VÂ» per cent,
the price dropped without a check, except for
momentary pause at each even number, to
110^4, which was 4*i per cent below the best.
Numerous blocks of many thousand shares
were thrown on the market with precipitate
eagernesÃ and the market speedily took on
panicky conditions. Rock Island foil In sym
pathy 3 points, Burlington 2 points, People's
Gas 24 and Northern Pacific preferred 2%.
The demoralization caused by the break in St.
Paul came on a market showing considerable
Strength and at a level materially above yes
terday's close. The rate for call money, after
folng to 4 per cent, eased off to 3 per cent un
er the influences of the engagements of |400 -
000 gold for Imports.
Bonds wore dull and firm most of the day
but yielded In the later dealings. Total sales,
12.900.0 W. United States old 4s, registered, the
8b and the 3f, when Issued, advanced Vi per cent
in the bid price.

LONDON MARKET.
NEW YORK. Sept. B.â The Evening Port's
London financial cablegram says: The mar
kets opened here flat to-day. Consols and
most other stocks were offered, but the fall
was most marked In Americans, which were
freely sold on the decline In New York ex
; change and on the belief that dearer money
!Is at hand In New York. The decline, how
! ever, was short-lived and the general smart
â  recovery whlrh has taken place all around
confirms yesterday's cabled statement aa to
the strong undertone of the markets.
The recovery in Americans was accompanied
by rumors that St. Paul will declare a $2 SO
dividend and asl bonus. Mainly on this report
and on the big arbitrage house picking up
i Etock prices closed firm.
A sharp drop In Portuguese securities on
| the Delagoa Bay report which Is still believed
in many quarters, and the upward movement in
Kaffirs were again features.
Canadian Pacific. 91%; Grand Trunk. 7%.
Bar silver steady, 27%. Money per
cent.

1 CHICAGO, Sept. S.â Heavy Northwestern re
| celpts of wheat, a knowledge of which was in
i possession of the traders before the session
opened, went a long way toward neutralizing
the firmness with which wheat started at Liv
i erpool. The recent heavy and quite steady dÂ»
! cllne and the comparatively low price to which
i wheat had already sunk, however, apparently
made friends for the grain and restrained the
ardor of the bears. Liverpool, notwithstanding
I a decline here, was Hd higher for September
! and Vn<l lower for December. Receipts con
; cernlng the Russian wheat crop were quite
bullish from a market point of view, as dlÂ«-
I appointing harvests were the general tenor.
! So far this season the movement of new wheat
! in Russia has been small enough to give what
I appears to be corroboratlon of the poor crop
1 reports from that country. Wheat futures
i were additionally strengthened near the close
! by a dispatch from New York to the effect
that Russian grain exporters who had soid
i wheat fro shipment In September to the United
â  Kingdom were trying to have the contracts
j canceled, presumably because of difficulty in
j getting the grain. The close was %<&%, c higher
for September and %<gHc for December.

Jacob Wollner's circular says: "The Wool
market Is still a puzzle, both here and in the
East. There are over fifteen million pounds of
Wool In this market, with none selling and
without Inquiry. Some little fall Wool is com
ing in, but it Is rather short In staple. It
really does not make much difference how
cheap Wool is offered, as there are no buyers,
and as there is very little woolen goods sell
ing manufacturers cannot get any orders.
They do not come Into the market to buy.
â¢'Something can be gleaned of the state of
the market in the East by the following stn
tlptics: The p.iles of last week in Boston
amounted to 1,700.000 pounds, against 11,700,
700 pounds for the corresponding week last
year. Snles since January 1 In Boston amounted
to 78.715.000 pounds, against 250,230,000 pounds
last year at this time.
"All the markets in the United States have a
large stock of Wool and there is a good deal
still In the hands of the growers. Still thÂ«
prices are well maintained, as when anybody
needs Wool and come into the market he Is
wllllnfr to pay full prices for what he wants,
but cannot be induced to buy more by getting
concessions."

THE STOCK MARKET.
There was a revival In Interest In mining
stocks yesterday, presumably owing to the
deep-mining agitation, and values were higher
all along the line, with Increased trading.
Con. Cal. & Va. advanced to 68c.
Local securities were In ordinary demand,
with no fluctuations worthy of special note.
The boards will not be In session to-day.

THE REVOLUTIONARY TORIES.
If George 111 and his Ministers were
embarrassed by oposition at home, the
American patriots were no less embar
rassed. An energetic minority, it has
been said, brought to pass the Revolu
tion, which proceeding, especially from
New England, was carried through in
spite of a majority in the coloniesâ a ma
jority in great part quite apathetic but
to some extent actively resisting. The
emigration of Tories, when the day was
at last won, was relatively as great as
that of the Huguenots from Prance after
the revocation of the Edict of Nantes.
The total number is estimated to have
been at least 100,000. In this multitude
were comprised only such, with their
families, as had been active for the King.
The indifferent, who had lent no helping
hand to the patriots, must have been a
multitude much larger; these remained
behind, inertly submitting to the new
order of things as they had swayed in
ertly this way or that, following the
power and direction of the blast of war.
âAtlantic Monthly^

NOTICE TO MARINERS.
A branch of the United States Hydrograph'.e
Office, located in the Merchants' Exchange, is
maintained in Pan Francisco for the benefit of
mariners, without regard to nationality and
free of expense.
Navigators are cordially Invited to visit the
office, where complete sets of charts and sail
ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, and the latest
Information can always be obtained regarding
lights, dangers to navigation and all matters
of Interest to ocean commerce.
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
building, at the foot of Market street, is hoi3ted
about ten minutes before noon and dropped at
neon, 120 th meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived each day from the United States Signal
Observatory, Mare Island, Cal.
A notice stating whether the ball was dropped
on time or giving the error, if any, is published
in the morning papers the following day.
CHAS. P. WELCH,
Ensign (retired), U. S. N., in charge.

NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given In the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day In the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column gives the second tide of
the day, the third time column the third tide
and the last or right hand column gives the
last tide of the day, except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
(riven are additions to the soundings on the
Ignited States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (â ) precedes the height,
and then the number given Is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference Is the mean of the lower low waters

a Wealthy Girl at Santa
Rosa and Is in
Jail.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, Sept. 8.
Two weeks ago Rosie Albrecht tiled a
suit for divorce from Charles Alfred Al
brecht in this county, and suit Is still
pending, as no one lias yet been able to
serve the summons on Albrecht. He was
married to Miss Mamie Boagard in De
cember of last year, and she is the lady
â¢who Is now desirous of being free.
This morning Attorney Leonard C.ark,
who Is Mrs. Albrecht'.s attorney, read a
dispatch from Santa Rosa, stating that
Charles Albrecht and Miss May Mar
shall, daughter of a well-known Sonoma
farmer, were married a few days ago,
and after their honeymoon returned to
Marshall's ranch. On their arrival Al
brecht was arrested on a charge of hav
ing obtained money through an alleged
fraudulent check.
Attorney Clark called on Chief of Po
lice Fletcher to-day and declared that
Albrecht Is a bigamist, and asked the
Chief to communicate with the Sheriff
of Sonoma County and have him detuin
cd on a charge of bigamy, with the privi
lege extended to Mr. Marshall to prose
cute if he felt so disposed.
A'brecht evidently has a system of In
ducing girls to marry- him. The published
etory from Santa Rosa compared very
favorably with the story Attorney Clark
says Albrecht told Miss Boagard when
proposing marriage to her last winter,
lie represented that he was wealthy, and
â took her to San Francisco, where he
â spent a few hundred dollars for a seal

Boapard told her parents that she feared
all was not right. To allay her trepida
tion he showed her an alleged forged tel
egram dated at San Matoo, telling of
his appointment to a lucrative position
4as an architect. lie induced her to ac
â  fompany him to San Mateo.
This was on December 20, and two
dayÃ Liter the young wife's sister took

Health Officer Dunn Swears to a
Complaint.
... OAKLAND, Sept. S.â Health Officer J.
yp. Dunn has finally obtained a warrant
for the. arrest of M. M. Samson, the butch
er who -supplied tainted meat to the sold
iers at Camp Barrett. The complaint was
sworn to by the Health Officer in Justice
Lawrence's . court, .Brooklyn : Township,
charging Samson with- misdemeanor, in
Belling, keeping: and offering for sale an
article of food, with intent to permit the
same to be eaten, knowing it had become
unfit by reason of disease to be eaten.
Constable Glaze took the warrant, which
will be served immediately.
Samson now states that he Is unable to
"say how' the diseased meat got to Camp
Barrett or who delivered it. He says that
. the Bercet of the mean scandal lies in. the.
fact that he represents one of two whole-
Bale .butcher associations and the union
represents the other.
