PLOTTED TO
BE DIVORCED
After the False Teeth of
His Rival.
WANTED A WATCH CHARM
LEWIS WAS DISARMED TO
AVOID A MURDER.
He Found His Wife With Another
Man and Was Led to the
Couple by His
Sister.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, Sept. 8.
This afternoon Attorney Tom Bradley
obtained from the Sheriff a pistol which
but for the timely work of a Deputy
Sheriff would have caused a tragedy at
the Chateau at Piedmont. The circum
stances connected with this pistol point to
a very clever plot by which a sister at
tempted to aid her brother In securing
a divorce from a wife who was not des
perately fond of him.
T. J. Lewis is the man who owns the
gun, and just what steps he will take
through his attorney, whether civil or
criminal, is not yet known. A few days
ago Lewis was in Colfax, when he re
ceived a letter from his sister telling him
that his wife was making appoint
ments with A. E. Harris, a local sales
man. Lewis, anxious for something
upon which to seek a separation, quick
ly returned to Oakland, and was inform
ed by his sister that Harris, Mrs. Lewis,
a man named David and Lewis' sister had
gone to the Chateau in Pleasant Valley.
Lewis at once loaded his pistol and went
to the Chateau with another man to get
his wife. He told Officer Hitchcock that
his desire was to secure a divorce, and
told him he preferred to use a club instead
of a pistol. The officer not only declined
to loan him his club, but took away the
gun. A few minutes later Lewis again
met the officer, told him that his wife
was not at the Chufau, and asked him to
return the gun. Hitchcock did so, and
Lewis returned toward the Chateau, at
the same time reloading his gun, which
Hitchcock had emptied. Seeing- this, he
was again disarmed, and he then ran to
the house and found his wife and Harris
in a dining room. The men grappled with
each other, and in the tight Miss Lewis
struck her brother over the head with a
bottle, but immediately saw her mistake,
and explained to him that she meant It
for Harris. After a while Harris broke
away, with the aid of the officer and the
restaurant keeper.
Lewis then told the crowd that he did
not intend to kill Harris, but he intended
to force him to his knees and make him
take out his false teeth, which Lewis de
clared he was going to hang on his watch
chain as a memento of the evening's
sport.
Lewis' sister and Mr. Randberger, who
had accompanied Lewis to the Chateau,
then started away, and a few minutes
later were calling for help, boih of them
having fallen into a sewer ditch six feet
deep. Lewis was around town to-day
looking for Harris, but the latter had
disappeared. Officer Hitchcock turned
the gun over to the Sheriff, and this af
ternoon it was delivered to Attorney
Bradley for his client..
THE JONES DIVORCE
HAS BEEN GRANTED
SEQUEL OF A SENSATIONAL
SERIES OF SCANDALS.
Continuation of the Disbarment Pro
ceedings in Which Hutchinson
Is Charged With Ut
tering Libel.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, Sept. S.
Judge Ogden to-day granted a divorce
to Gertrude Jones from Ernest Norwood
Jones on the ground of infidelity, at the
same time awarding plaintiff custody of
the two minor children, all of the prop
erty and $50 per month alimony for the
support of herself and children. The
defendant is permitted to visit the chil
dren at such times as may be convenient
to plaintiff. The parties were married in
San Francisco April 3. 1894.
Indirectly the case has some connection
with the disbarment proceedings brought
by Attorneys Henry N. Beatty and
George H. Francoeur against Attorney
Joseph Hutchinson, which was resumed
in Judge Hall's court this morning, Hut
chinson having acted a-s Mrs. Jones' at
torney in the suit of Francoeur against
Mrs. Jones for the recovery of $500 on a
promissory note given In payment as at
torney's fees.
The petitioners for Hutchinson's dis
barment allege that the latter in the
answer to the suit on the note made
statements which he knew to oe scan
dalous and libelous regarding them.
Henry Beatty was on the stand to-day,
and his testimony in the main was a de
nial of the charges made by Hutchinson
as attorney for Mrs. Jones in the suit
brought by Francoeur to recover attor
ney's fees for the defense of Jack How
ard when the last mentioned was under
charge of murder in the celebrated Annie
Johnson case, in connection with which
case E. N. Jones' name had been mention
ed. Beatty admitted that he had paid
Harvard $10 per week for several months
on behalf of Jones, and that after the
murder trial Francoeur had paid Jack
Howard and Hulda Johnson $75 as a pres
ent. It was shown that lteatty and
Francoeur had been retained to defend
Hulda Johnson and Jack Howard, evi
dently at the solicitation of Jones.
Witness Hfatty asserted that he had
never toTd Jones that money would be re
quired in order to keep the papers from
publishing the details of the Johnson
murder, and he had never represented to
Mrs. Jones that there was any fear of her
husband'a arrest at any time. Hutchin
son had told Mrs. Jones this. Witness
denied that Mrs. Jones over asked him
for money, but said that Jones told him
that Mrs. Johnson was after money.
Beatty was asked how much money he
received Jn the defense of the case, and
he replied $1191, of which he retained $^s<i,
the rest being paid over to other claim
ants. Witness had not heard from Jones
for a long time, but had read in to-day's
papers of his arrest in the East on a
felony charge.
Mrs. Jones was also on the stand to
day testifying that her husband was out of
thi's State since last February; that she
was In the hospital last May. when the
answer In the Francoeur suit was brought
to her for her signature.
The petitioners claim to be able to
prove that Mrs. Jones was never consult
ed regarding a defense to the note suit
until after answer was served on her;
that she never appeared before a notary
and acknowledged to the scandalous al
legations in the answer.
Further hearing in the motion went
over until Tuesday morning at 10 o'clock.
The Thayer Inquest.
HERKELEY, Sept. B.â The inquest held
last night over the remains of the lato
William Parker Thayer. who shot himself
Tuesday, returned a verdict of Buicide.
The jury's finding was not, however,
unanimous. Dr. John G. Jessup held the
opinion that "death was caused by a
gunshot wound, accidentally inflicted,"
and brought in a minority report to that
effect. The verdict was signed by seven
jurors.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, FRIDAY, SEPTEMBER 9, 1898.
BOYS PLAY
WITH FIRE
Piedmont Springs in
Grave Danger.
MAKE FULL CONFESSION
STRUCTURES ARE SAVED BY
MUCH HABD WORK.
President Havens Appealed To on
Behalf of the Tibbitts Boys,
Who Fired the
Underwood -
Oakland Office Son Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, Sept. 8.
Two little boys named Tibbltts are
anxiously waiting for the return of Presi
dent Havens of the Piedmont Springs
property. When Ar. Havens returns he
may decide to prosecute the lade for
starting three fires on the property sim
ultaneously and exposing valuable build
ings to great risk merely for fun.
The boys have signed a complete state
ment of the Incidents connected with the
fires at Piedmont Springs, which wore
only put out after a hard battle waged by
a gang of men. The little fellows were
coming from school, and after taking a
bath in the reservoir they decided to
light a fire for amusement. When they
had lit one and it had got a good headway
they left it and started another among
the deep dry undergrowth surrounding
the building. This pleased them so much
that they started still another, and when
the flames almost met they became
frightened and tried to put them out.
The dense smoke attracted the attention
of a special officer, and as it was seen
that the buildings were in danger he tele
phoned to headquarters and was told to
organize a band of men at once and put
the tires out.
It required the services of half a dozen
men for some hours to prevent an ex
pensive conflagration as a result of the
boys' mischief. An investigation was
made, and the little culprits, who ran off
as soon as they saw the danger, were
easily found. They have since written out
a full account of the affair for President
Havens, and their parents have entered a
plea for mercy, which will probably be
granted.
EL NIDO CLUB SUITS.
All the Members Who Were in Good
Standing Are Sued for the
Club's Indebtadness.
ALAMEDA, Sept. B.â The last chapter
in the history of the unfortunate El Nido
Club was opened to-day, when I. P. Allen,
assignee of the club, which is insolvent,
filed suits In Justice Morris' court against
all the members who were in good stand
ing at the time the organization went into
insolvency, for the purpose of collecting
the sum of 1965 76. or $15 09 from each In
dividual. Sepacate suits were filed
against each member. The complaints al
lege that on or about August 3, 1898, the
said El Nido Club was declared insolvent
In the Superior Court of this county and
I. P. Allen was appointed assignee of the
estate thereof. That the club Is indebted
to divers and sundry creditors In the sum
of ${*>5 70. and each member is sued for his
pro rata of that ambunt.
El Nido Club had a flourishing existence
far a brief period. It included many of
the prominent business men of the city,
but finally some of the members ran up
bills which they did not pay, chiefly for
liquid refreshments, and when the club
was called on for settlement It had no
money in the treasury.
. particularly aggravating feature of
the affair is that most of the men who
have been sued are of the number who
paid their bills and dues regularly and
are substantial business men, while those
who ate and drank on credit were dropped
from membership and their associates are
now called upon to pay for their refresh
ments.
GOLDEN WEDDING.
Celebrated Last Evening by Mr. and
Mrs. Stephen Fletcher of
Alameda.
ALAMEDA, Sept. B.â Mr. and Mrs. Ste
phen Fletcher of this city celebrated their
golden wedding anniversary last evening
at their residence, 2255 Central avenue. A
notable feature of the gathering was the
presence of Mrs. Charles Dickinson, who
was the bridesmaid at the ceremony fifty
years ago, when the couple were married
in South Boston, Mass. Twelve of the
seventeen grandchildren of the couple
were present, with one great-grandchild
also in attendance. During the evening
several hundred friends of the couple,
who are still hale and hearty, gathered
to greet them and bestow con
gratulations upon the happy pair.
Manw gifts were received, and letters
and telegrams of congratulation from
distant localities were received. The
rooms had been very prettily decorated
by Miss Lucy Slosson, while refreshments
were served by Mrs. D. H. Weeks, Mrs.
Slosson, Mrs. M. French, Mrs. Captain
Porter, Dr. M. E. Ritter and Miss M. E.
Palmer.
Four daughters of the couple assisted
In receiving callers. They were: Dr. Mary
Fletcher, Mrs. C. K. Cadman, Mrs. J. G
Cadman and Mrs. Douglas Kelley. Dur
ing the afternoon Bishop Nichols called
and gave his blessing to the aged couplo.
Mr. Fletcher is well-known in San
Francisco business circles, he having been
for over twenty years collector for the
California Wire Works Company.
Alameda City Trustees.
ALAMEDA. Sept. S.â At the meeting of
the Board of Trustees last evening one
of the hose companies presented a re
quest for a gift of half a dozen fire hats
It was suggested that as the companies
had Just divided up the relief fund, re
ceiving over $300 each, It would be as well
for them to buy their own hats. The
board thereupon refused the request.
The manager of the Odorless Garbage
Crematory made application for permis
sion to make a test of their new works
on the marsh near the Webster street
bridge. There was considerable discus
sion, and at length the board granted the
request. Trustee Brick voting no. They
will be permitted to burn fifty tons of
Alameda garbage in order to see whether
the crematory is objectionable or not.

The Dean Forgery.
OAKLAND. Sept. B.â The Grand Jury
resumed its session this afternoon, con
tinuing its inquiry into the alleged regis
tration frauds done in the County Clerk's
office during the recent Congressional
primaries. Expert E. J. Dunshee was one
of the principal witnesses before the jury
this afternoon and testified that the Dean
signature was an absolute forgery.
The Grand Jury had been advised that
in the absence of any primary law there
was grave doubt whether there could be
any crime and that the investigation could
hardly result in the return of an indict
ment. The matter Is therefore understood
to be dropped.

Sold Goods Under Weight.
BERKELEY, Sept. B.âSeveral "West
Berkeley gTocers are lamenting a swin
dle operated upon them this morning by
two agents selling cheese. The men wero
altogether unknown in this vicinity, but
their affable manners charmed away all
suspicion. They found ready sales
throughout the town. But one or two
of the dealers on weighing their goods
found them to be several pounds under
weight. It was then discovered that ev
ery grocer who had purchased of the
strangers had been swindled. The bills
made out for each -sale all bear the head
ing: "Marshfield Cheese Factory, Manu
facturers of Martin's New York Cheese.
Factory, Marshfleld, Oregon." The police
have been notified, and they are now
looking for the offenders.
WANTS HIS PRESENT BACK.
Francisco Rossi Brings Suit to Re-
cover a Wedding Gift to His
Daughter.
Francisco Rossi brought suit against
his former son-in-law. Carlo Barabino,
and Barabino's divorced wife, Maria,Ros
si's daughter, in Justice Kerrigan's court
yesterday to recover $150 which he had
given to the young couple in order to fa
cilitate their wedding several years ago.
Not long ago the wife secured a divorce
from Barabino, whereupon Rossi was
wroth and determined to recover the wed
ding dowry In the courts.
Barabino testified that the father of the
bride had offered him $1000 to marry his
daughter, but had only paid $150. This
the father denied. The Justice continued
the case, so as to have time to determine
whether a cash bridal present can be- re
covered by the father of the bride when
divorce occurs.
GOLF MEETING ON
SAN RAFAEL LINKS
THE BIGGEST TOURNAMENT OF
THE SEASON.
A Two Days' Programme of Events
on the San Rafael -md Oak
land Grounds.
To-day will be a great day for the golf
ers. At San Rafael is an open handicap
tournament and at Oakland i 6 a ladles'
tournament. These two events will draw
all the golfing enthusiasts about the bay
as some very good tournament playing is
expected.
The programme at San Rafael is by far
the most promising for sport. T. \V. Tut
tle and the management of the Hotel Ra
fael have arranged the tournament to
which every golf player in the State has
been invited. The San Rafael Golf Club
will not be entirely organized until Oc
tober 1, but this tournament is held with
their sanction and with their assistance.
The programme extends over two days.
To-day there will be five events, which
are as follows:
Boys' and girls' competition (not over 16
years) at 9 a. m.
Ladies' handicap competition, nine holes, at
10:30 a. m.
Men's competition, 75-yard approach, at 11:30
a, m.
Mr-- s competition, open, 18 holes, at 1 p. m.
Ladles' competition, driving-, at 4:30 p. m.
The tournament will be finished to-mor
row, when the best golfers of the State
will compete. The events are:
Ladles' competition, open 9 holes, 10:30 a. m. !
Men's competition, driving, 11:30 a. m.
Men's handicap competition, 18 holsa, at 1:30
p. m.
Ladies' competition, 80-yard approach, at 4:30
p. m.
Distribution of prizes at 6 p. m.
On the Oakland links there will be a compe
tition for the silver gift cup, which was won
last June, and muot bo contested for again.
The following are entered for the competition:

Rural Free Delivery.
A. B. Smith, Special' Agent Rural Free
delivery, arrived from Washington yes
terday for the purpose of investigating
and reporting upon the rural free delivery
system Inaugurated at Berryessa, Santa
Clara County. This Is the only rural dis
trict in the State In which a free delivery
has been established. An application for
similar facilities has been made for the
colonies adjoining the city of Fresno, and
it is likely that Mr. Smith will be in
structed to pay a visit to that thriving
part of the State.
Mr. Smith is proprietor of the Herald at
Martlnsburg, W. va.
Democrats Organize.
The James O. Maguire Democratic Club
of the Forty-first Assembly District was
organized last night at Sinons Hall. The
following ..officers were elected:
President, William P. Humphreys; first
vice-president, Dr. T. L. Mahoney; second
vice-president, Charles W. Hayden; finan
cial secretary, Bernard Hyland; corre
sponding secretary. Edward Convey Jr.;
treasurer, P. J. Thomas; sergeant-at
arms, Dennis Sullivan; executive com
mittee, A. J. Vanderwhite, James Furey,
Frank H. Kilduff, John H. Brickwedel
and John N. Much.
Baldwin Loses a Suit.
E. S. Joy, assignee of W W. Montague
& Co., was given judgment yesterday
against E. J. Baldwin for $3355 37. Bald
win purchased several bills of hardware
from W. W. Montague & Co., and on hie
failure to pay the bill it was assigned to
iuq olalntiff, who recovered Judgment.

PROCLAMATION.
STATE OF CALIFORNIA,
EXECUTIVE DEPARTMENT,
SACRAMENTO, July 30th, 1898.
WHEREAS, The Legislature of the State of
California at Its thirty-second session begin-
ning on the fourth dÂ»y of January. A. D. 1897.
two-third* of ail the members elected to each
cf the two houses of said Legislature voting
In favor fliereof, proposed the following: de-
scribed amendments to the Constitution of tho
Btate of California, to-wlt:
AMENDMENT NUMBER ONE.
(Being Senate Constitutional Amendment No.
41.)
A resolution to propose to the people of the
Btate of California an amendment to section
eighteen of article eleven of the Constitu-
tion, In .relation to revenue and taxation, by
which it te proposed to amend said section to
read as follows:
Section 18. No county, city, town, township,
board of education or school district shall In-
cur any Indebtedness or liability in any man-
ner or for any purpose exceeding in any year
the income and revenue provided for it for
such year, without the assent of two-thirds
of the qualified electors thereof, voting at an
election to be held for that purpose, nor un-
less before, or at the time of Incurring such
Indebtedness, provision shall be made for the
collection of an annual tax sufficient to pay
the Interest on such Indebtedness as it falls
due, and also provision to constitute a sinking
fund for the payment of the principal thereof
on or before maturity, which shall not exceed
forty years from the time of contracting the
same; provided, however, that the City and
County of San Francisco may at any time pay
the unpaid claims with Interest thereon for
materials furnished to and work done for said
city and county during the forty-third and
forty-fourth fiscal years, out of the Income and
revenue of any succeeding year or years; pro-
vided, that any and all claims for making, re-
pairing, altering or for any work done upon or
for any material furnished for any Etreet. lane,
alley, court, place or sidewalk, or for the con-
struction of any sev/er or sewers in said city
and county are hereby cx-epted from the pro-
visions of this section; am In determining any
claim permitted to be paid by this section, no
statute of limitations shall apply In any man-
ner: and provided further, that the City of
Vallejo, in Polano County, may pay its exist-
ing Indebtedness incurred in the construction
Of Its water works, whenever two-thirds of the
electors thereof voting at an election held for
that purpose shall so decide. Any indebted-
ness or liability incurred contrary to this pro-
vision with the exception hereinbefore recited,
shall be void.
AMENDMENT NUMBER TWO.
(Being Senate Constitutional Amendment No.
10.)
A resolution proposing to the people of the
Btate of California an amendment t the Con-
stitution of the State, by adding a new sec-
tion, to be known and designated aÂ« section
â even and one-half, article eleven thereof, pro-
viding for the framing by the Inhabitants of
counties of local county government acts for
their own government. The said proposed new
section to read as follows:
Section 7V4. The Inhabitants of any county
may frame a county government act for their
own government, relating to the matters here-
inafter specified, and consistent with and sub-
ject to the Constitution and laws of this State,
by causing a board of fifteen freeholders, who
have been, for at leaÃt five years, qualified
electors of such county, to be elected by the
qualified electors of such county, at any gen-
eral or special election, whose duty It shall be
within ninety days after such election, to pre-
pare and propose a county government act for
euch county, which shall be signed In dupli-
cate by the members of surh board, or a ma-
jority of them, and returned, one copy thereof
to the Board of Supervisors or other legisla-
tive body of such county, and the other copy
to be sent to the Recorder of Deeds of the
county Such proposed county government act
shall then be published in two papers of gen-
eral circulation In such county, or If there be
not two such papers, then in one only, for at
least twenty days, and within not less than
thirty days after such publication it shall be
submitted to the qualified electors of such
county, at a general or special election, and if
a majority of such qualified electors voting
thereon shall ratify the same, It shall there-
after be submitted to the Legislature for. its
rejection or approval, as a whole, without '
power of alteration or amendment, and If ap-
proved by a majority of the members elocted
to each house, it shall be the county govern-
ment act of such county, and shall In such
caÂ«e become the organic law thereof and super-
sede any existing county government act, and
ail amendments thereof, and all special laws
Inconsistent with such county government act.
A copy of such county government act, certi-
fied by the President of the Board of Super-
visors or other legislative body of such county,
and authenticated by the seal of such county,
getting forth the submission of such county
government act to tho electors, and its ratifica-
tion by them, shall be made In duplicate and
deposited, one In the office of the Secretary of
Ptate the other, after being recorded In the
office' of the Recorder of Deeds In the county,
among the archives of the county.
All courts shall take Judicial notice thereof.
The county government act so ratified may be
amended at intervals of not Jess than two
years by propocnls therefor, submitted by the
legislative authority of the county, to the
ounllfied electors thereof, at a general or spe-
cial election held at least forty days after the
fmbllcatlon of such proposals for twenty days
n a newspaper of general circulation in such
county and ratified by at least three-fifths of
the a'ualifled electors voting thereon, and ap-
proved by the Legislature as herein provided
for the approval of the county government act.
In submitting any such courity governmont act
any alternative article or proposition may be
nresented for the choice of the voters, and may
br voted on separately without prejudice to
Â° rt^shall b* competent In all county govern-
ment' act? framed under the authority given by
this section, to provide for the manner In
which the times at which, and the terms for
which 'the several township and county officers
other than Judges of the Pupertor Court, shall
be elected or appointed: for thÂ»tr compensa-
tion- for the number of such officers, for the
consolidation or segregation of offices, for the
number of deputies that each officer shall have,
and for the compensation payable to each of
Biirh deputies, fcr the manner In which, the
times in which, and the terms for which the
numbers of all boards of election shall be
elected or appointed and for the constitution,
reflation compensation and government of
such boards, and of their clerks and attaches;
also to prescribe the manner and method by
which all election* by the people shall be con-
ducted- and may In addition determine the
tests and conditions upon which electors, po-
litical parties and organizations may partici-
pate in any primary election.
Whenever nny ecunty has, in the manner
and method herein pointed out. adopted nny
county government act. and the same shall
have been approved by the Legislature as
aforesaid the direction of sections four and
five of this article providing for the uniformity
of a system of county governments throughout
the State and llkewise'iprovlding for the elec-
tion and appointment of officers, and the regu-
lation of their compensation, shall not apply.
Paid county government act shall, as to any of
the matters herelnabove provided for and de-
clared by such county government act. not be
subject to any law or amendment enacted by
the Legislature, except by amendment first
submitted to the electors and ratified In the
manner herelnabove set forth.

AMENDMENT NUMBER SIX.
(Being Assembly Constitutional Amendment
No. 38.)
A resolution to propose to the people of tha
State of California an amendment to section
Blx, article nine of the Constitution of the Stata
of California, relating to grammar schools, by
which it Is proposed to amend said section to
read as follows:
Section 6. The public school system shall in-
clude primary and grammar schools, and such
n.gh schools, evening schools normal school*
and technical schools as may be established by
the Legislature or by municipal or district
authority, but the entire revenue derived from
the State school fund and the State school tax
shall be applied exclusively to the Bupport of
primary and grammar schools. Grammar
schools shall Include schools organized in a,
school district, or union of school districts,
having more than one thousand inhabitants, in
which a course of study shall be taught which
will prepare pupils to enter the agricultural
mining or scientific department of the Univer-
sity of California.
AMENDMENT NUMBER SEVEN.
(Being Assembly Constitutional Amendment
No. 34.)
A resolution to propose to the people of tha
State of California an arrendroent to section
two of article four of the Constitution, In rela-
tion to sessions of the Legislature, by which It
is proposed to amend said section to read as
follows:
Section 2. The sessions of the Legislature
Fhall commence at twelve o'clock meridian on
the first Monday after the first day of January
next succeeding the election of its members
and shall be biennial unless the Governor shall
In the interim convene the Legislature by proc-
lamation. The Legislature shall then remain
In session for twenty-five days, after which it
must adjourn to some date not less than thirty
nor more than sixty days from the time of ad-
journment. If the two houses fail to acres
upon a time at which they will resume their
session, the Governor shall, by proclamation,
fix a date for such reconvening, which shall ba
within the limits abore prescribed. Upon re-
assembling the Legislature shall complete its
session. No pay shall be allowed to members
for a longer period than seventy-five days and
no bill shall be introduced in either house ex-
cept at the first twenty-five days of the session
without the consent of three-fourths of tha
members thereof.

FOUR FAVORITES
CAPTURE RACES
Good Sport at State Fair
Meeting-.
BIG CROWDS AT THE TRACK
PIGGOTT THE STAB HORSE PILOT
AT SACRAMENTO.
Bides Four Winners and a Third
Horse in the Five Race*
Where He Haw
Mounts.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SACRAMENTO, Sept. B.â The racing
steadily improves as the meeting wears
on, and the same can also be said of
the attendance. The latter was placed
at 4000 to-day. The balance was on
the right side of the ledger of the pub
lic, for four favorites and two well
played second choices got home In
front. Especially heavy was the bet
ting on Harry Thoburn in the last
event, and this time he did not disap
point his admirers, though Moringa was
the best horse in the race. It is a fact
worth knowing that the winners, Jen-
nie Reid and Scarborough, are brother
and sister. Piggott rode four winners
and a third horse in the five races
where he had mounts, and is easily the
star horse pilot here. Bullman ap
pears to be a comer.
Midlove was made a slight favorite
over Jennie Reid for the Shafter sell-
Ing stake, six furlongs, for which five
lined up against the rubber. To a good
Btart, Jennie Reid going best, led the
band for over a furlong, then Midlove
headed her and led past the half. The
Miller now ran up very fast and led by
the saddle skirts into the homestretch.
Jennie Reid, under vigorous riding, got
up to the leaders less than half-way
down the straight and won easily by
two lengths; Casdale second as far be
fore Nilgar, who had made up a vast
amount of territory in the last three
furlongs. Midlove, the favorite, wound
up absolutely last. Time, 1:16&.
A mile selling race came next, and
It had four starters and Shasta Water
for a 4 to 5 favorite. Cromwell was at
6 to 5, Joe Terry 6 to 1 and Kruna 8 to
I (played from 12). Kruna, a good
looking bay mare, led for about a fur
long, then Shasta Water sailed away
to the fore, leading to the home stretch
by from half a length to a length,
Cromwell second most of the way. Un
der Piggott's vigorous riding Cromwell
beaded the Burns & Waterhouse mare
half way down the straight and won
cleverly by two lengths, Shasta Water
eecond, six lengths from Joe Terry, who
made up some ground from the half to
the stretch. Time, 1:42.
San Mateo was a 1 to 4 shot in the
third race, six and a half furlongs, sell
ing. Major Cook was second In demand
at 6 to 1, the others 10 to 20 to 1. Off to
a fair start. Major Cook took the lead
and held it past the quarter by saddle
skirts, at the half by his head; then
the slow-to^get-moving San Mateo said
"Ta-ta, Major," and leading into the
homestretch by four lengths won in a
canter, eased up, by two and a half
lengths, Major Cook a handy second,
two lengths before Lady Britannic, the
other two back a short block. Time,
1:22 Mi.
The fourth race, five furlongs for
maiden two-year-olds, brought six to
the rubber band. Almoneer and Fa
versham were at 8 to 5 each, Cleodora
B to 1, Napian and Crossmolina 5 to 1,
Alcatraz 6, latter two played rather
Btrongly. The start was poor. Alcatraz
and Cleodora might as well have been

Appolonia Koch's Will.
The wHI of Appolonla Koch, who died
on the 27th inst., was filed for probate
yesterday. The sum of $2000 is bequeath
ed the executors of the estate, Andrew
Grass and Konrad Meussdorffer, in trust
for Alice Grass a niece of the testatrix,
and $200 annually in trust for her educa
tion and maintenance. The personal
property of the deceased is given Elisa
betha Grass, her sister. The residue of
the estate is bequeathed In equal shares
to the mother, sisters and brothers of the
testatrix.

Bogus Advertising Contract.
L. C. Ewell, an advertising agent at 43
S'.xth street, swore to a complaint in
Judge Joachimsen's court yesterday for
the arrest of Steve Vulfcevich on a charge
Of petty larceny by trick and device, but
a more serious charge may be preferred
against him. He went to San Jose a few
days ago and returned to Kwcll with a
contract purporting to be signed by Jlin
Rea for $150 for advertising in connection
with the campaign. The contract waj in
dorsed with an order upon Colonrl Dan
Burns for the money, but Burns rofuse-1
to recognize the order. Meantime Ewell
had advanced Vulicevich $10 upon the
contract, believing it to be genuine. It
now depends upon Ttea if a charge of for
gery will bo made against Vulicevlca.

MRS. ROTHKUFF SAW MYSTERIOUS
WOMAN AT THE FERRY POSTAL STATION
FEMESSEFS
STRANGE STORY
â¢ CORROBORATED
Sylvia Heney Says She
Sold the Candy.
SHE IDENTIFIES THE BOX
NEW DEVELOPMENTS IN THE
DUNNING TRAGEDY."
Legal Complications That May Pre
vent Mrs. Botkin From Re
ceiving a Fair
Trial.
Another rpmr.rk.ible coincidence that
may assume Importance for the defenders
of Mrs. Cordelia Botkin h:is forced itself
out of the labyrinth of facts and fancies
In which the whole mysterious affair '8
clouded. Another woman has appeared
with information that Is certainly Inter
esting and may be of moment in the case.
Her contribution to the confusion of
theories is in corroboration of the asser
tion of J. IE. Fennessee that he saw a
woman resembling Mrs. Botkin post a
package shortly before 6 o'clock on the
afternoon of August 4 at the ferry postal
station.
This new witness is Mrs. Lena Roth
kuff. She does not know Mrs. Botkin,
and is not sure that h>-r testimony is of
any value. She simply describes what
Bhe saw and feels that, while she wishes
to avoid notoriety, her experience was
somewhat remarkable in conjunction with
other circumstances in the web of testi
mony. She also saw a woman post a
package at the ferry postal station a few
minutes before 6 o'clock on the afternoon
of August 4. The package. Mrs. Roth
kuff says, resembled the wrapped box of
poisoned candy. The woman who posted
It was very like the description published
of Mrs. Botkin. The Incident Is signifi
cant, as Mrs. Botkin can prove that she
was well on her way to St. Helena at the
time her double appeared at the ferry de
pot in this city.
Mrs. Lena Rothkuff Is a resident of
Sacramento. She was in this city on
August 4 on business connected with the
preparations for the Irish Fair. Having
< oncluded her mission Mrs. Rothkuif
started for the ferry to return to her
home. She intended to take the 6 o'cloc*
boat from this city, but lound, upon her
arrival at the ferry depot, that she was
mare than an hour too early.
Having nothing else to do she bupled
herself in watching the faces and follow
ing the movements of the hurrying crowds
of men and women who came ajid went
to and fro in the ferry building. While
occupied with this diversion Mrs. Roth
kuff says sho noticed particularly the ac
tions of .a woman that approached the
package window of the postal station,
then went away and again returned. The
â¢watcher's attention was specially at
tracted by the incidents as one or two
scenes at the ferry postoffice had already
interested her. A girl had entered hur
riedly with a letter in hor hand. She
kissed the epistle and then dropped it
into the mail. Mrs. Rothkuff watched to
see what the next visitor would do.
A short woman, not unlike Mrs. Bot
kin. entered the urea in front of the post
office. She carried a wrapped box that
In size arid shape resembled a box of
candy such ;;s dealers sell for 50 cents.
It was shortly before 6 o'clock and there
wore two or three men In front of the
postoffice. Mrs. Rr.thkuff says she did
not notice them particularly, and as far
as she knows one of them may have been
J. K. Fennessee. Her attention was fixed
upon the woman who had approached the
package window of the postoffice. The
Btranger hesitated a moment when she
reached the window. She made a mo
tton as If to retire for a few steps and
then returned to drop the package In the
mail.
A few minutes later Mrs. Rothkuff was
on her way to Sacramento. She thought
no more of the Incident at the ferry pos
tal station and dismissed it from her mind
as an incident in an idle diversion. When
the statement of J. E. Fennessee was pub
lished, however, Mrs. Rothkuff recalled
her experience. She told it to her frierds,
but look no steps to communicate with the
authorities. She can give no more par
ticular description of the strange woman
than that already indicated. The woman
with the package may have worr> glasses
as far as Mrs. Rothkuff knows, as the
watcher at the ferry did not look critically
at her face.
The facts that Mrs. Rothkuff has Intro
duced Into thr case nre of great impor
tance to tho defense In any plan to estab
lish for Mrs. Botkin an alibi. If it can be
shown that another woman, resembling
the accused, rruiiled a package In any way
like the box of poisoned candy a serious
doubt will be interjected to interfere with
the case of the prosecution. When Fen
nessee mad" his statement no greater Im
portance than that of a coincidence was
attached to it. Both the prosecution and
the defense saw its meaning, which Is now
greatly enhanced by the recital of Mrs.
Rotbkuff.
Mrs. Rothkuff was in the city yesterday
afternoon, but returned at night to ht?r
home in Sacramento. In an interview yes
terday she described the incident as al
ready outlined. The defense will undoubt
edly use her as a witness when the case
of Airs. Botkin is presented.
The police have given up all hope of
tracing the postage stamps on the box of
poisoned candy to their purchaser. It is
Impossible to locate so small a purchase,
and the endeavor will be given up as
hopeless. The inquiry before the local
Grand Jury has also ended. The police
authorities of this city do not wish to
injure their own plan of sending Mrs.
Botkin out of the State. If the inquiry
before the Grand Jury were to proceed
an Indictment might be the result, and
the prosecution would find itself in a
peculiar predicament. It was out of the
question for the police to use the offi
cial Inquisitors simply as agents to ob
tain testimony that could not be secured
in any other way. Little has been left

Mrs. Lena Rothknff, Who Introduces a New Mystery
in the Dunning Tragedy.
therefore except to put In legal form the
evidence that- has already been gathered.
This will be done as a preparation for the
extradition hearing before Governor
Budd. This proceeding has been set for
next Tuesday morning, and will be held
at the Palace Hotel.
Another saleswoman, employed by
George Haas, made a statement yester
day to the police. She Is Miss Sylvia
Heney, vA\o works as an "extra" clerk
and on occasions at night. The authori
ties are now convinced that it was she
and not Miss Kmma Herber who sold
the box of candy that was subsequently
poisoned. Both Miss Herber and Miss
Heney sold boxes of candy answering the
description of that which plays so prom
inent a part in the Dover tragedy. Miss
Herber told her story to the police, and
attempted, without success, to identify
Mrs. Botkin as the purchaser of the box.
After this failure Miss Heney was
brought forward. The young lady was
summoned to police headquarters last
night and made a complete statement.
She says that on the afternoon of July 31
she sold the box of candy which was sent
to Delaware. Her recital of the Incident
of the sale and the actions of the pur
chaser Is identical with that made by
Ml.sb Herber and already published in The
Call. Miss Heney can go further than
Miss Herber, however, as she explains
why she identifies the box.
The woman who made the purchase had
asked for a special box. With a desire
to sell a faded box and please her em
ployer Miss Heney chose that which she
Identified yesterday. An attempt was
then made to have the girl Identify Mrs.
Botkin. The young lady was taken to the
window of the matron's room, but Mrs.
Botkin retired from view. Another effort
will be made at the earliest possible mo-
imnt.
The police have also received the report
of T. Kytka, the handwriting expert who
is employed by the Police Department.
The expert solemnly announces that Mrs.
Botkln undoubtedly wrote the anonymous
letters to Mrs. Dunning and also the ad
dress on the box of poisoned candy. This
decision, coming from an employe of the
Police Department, was not unexpected.
An interesting legal question has arisen
in the case of Mrs. Cordelia Botkin which
will probably puzzle not only the lawyers
engaged on both sides but also Chief Lees,
who claims to be the leading counsel for
the State.
The constitutions of the State of Califor
nia and Delaware, as well as the constitu
tion' of the United States, provide that in
criminal prosecutions the accused shall be
confronted with the witnesses against him
or her In open court. The principle enun
ciated Is as old as the Magna Charta, It
was formulated for the purpose of pro
tecting persons accused of crime against
secret trials and the conspiracies of those
in power, who, in monarchical govern
ments, arbitrarily made way with their
political enemies.
In the Botkin case the question Is now
asked: How can the alleged poisoner be
confronted with the witnesses against her
if ehe is taken to Delaware, or how can
the same privilege be extended to*her if
she is tried here? There Is no process by
which the California witnesses can be
compelled to attend court In Delaware,
and there Is no process by which those In
that State can be brought here.
The constitutions of both States, as well
as the Federal organic law, guarantee
Mrs. Botkin the same privilege employed
by the prosecution. That is, she is en
titled to have witnesses in her defense
brought into court. If it should appear
that a person In Delaware refused to at
tend when subpenaed In her behalf she
could not constitutionally be convicted,
and if she were extradited on Governor
Budd'a warrant and her California wit
nesses should refuse to go to Delaware,
she could not be convicted there.
"The fact of this matter Is," said an'at
torney who has studied the rase, " Mrs.
Botkin's situation is an anomaly in the
law. Even If she were a fugitive from
Justice and could be extradited under the
Federal statute, none of the California
witnesses could be taken to Delaware
against her will. The common law pro
vided that when a crime was committed
partly in one and partly in another juris
diction it might be tried In either, but it
was always assumed that tne trial would
take place where the witnesses were lo
cated.
"If Mrs. Botkin actually was a fugitive
from Justice from Delaware the witnesses
for and against her would probably be
In the latter State; but as she Is not in
the legal sense a fugitive â never having
been lji Delaware â it happens that both
the witnesses for and against her are
mostly here.
"1 have no Idea how the prosecution in
the Bctkln case will get over this diffi
culty. Dunning is one of the important
wltnepses for the State. It will probably
be difficult to make out a case afrainst
her without his evidence. Suppose he
should refuse to come to California in
case she is tried here,*or go to Delaware
In case ehe Is extradited. I know of no
way . in which he could be compelled to
attend either jurisdiction."
The point here involved is Booner or
later bound to puzzle the lawyers on both
Bides. IndÂ«*d, it may cut an important
figure In thg argument before Governor
Budd on Tuesday next.

prosecution's evidence the accused woman
remains unattached, and the past two
days have been largely spent in hearing
the numerous witnesses who were sup
posed to have been familiar with Mrs.
Miss Grace Allison, Who Posed as & Rich Widow, and H. C.

Botkin's movements, particularly with
her shopping tours.
Of these A. B. Barnes, proprietor of
the Hotel Victoria, one time the abiding

He was called Into the presence of Chief
Lees yesterday and subjected to a rigid
interrogation, but as It had not been nls
custom to record the doings of his guests
the Information that was had of him was
only of a general character.
Mrs. Botkin's remaining days in jnil
will be probably not so devoid of event
as have those of the two weeks past. The
procession of freaks who are morbidly at
tracted by great crimes and great crim
inals has begun, and the prisoner Is kept
busy declining to be exhibited. One party
oT six simpering young things were
taught a lesson yesterday that should be
of lasting benefit. Armed with a permit
from the Chief, thpy descended upon the
City Prison and insistently demanded that
the prisoner be dragged rorth. The pris
oner declined to be dragged, but the de
mands were not squelched until Turnkey
Shaw ordered them out of the prison.

Murderer Found Insane.
A Jury in Judge Wallace's court yes
terday, under advice of the court, return
ed a verdict in the case of Henry "Wil
liams, the aged pauper who killed Louis
Perrllo, a fellow-inmate of the Alms
house, finding the defendant at present
insane. He was committed to the asy
lum at Ukiah. Should he recover he will
be tried for murder. He is 76 years old.
WANTED MONEY
ALONG WITH
HIS BRIDE
H. C. Franks Was a
Soft, Easy Victim.
V
A MATRIMONIAL AGENCY FAKE
ANSWERED AN ADVERTISE
MENT OF A "RICH WIDOW."
She Got Him on a String, Then
Worked Him for Costly Pres
ents â Now She Is
in Jail.
Men who seek a sharer of their joys
and Borrow^, through the medium of a
matrimonial agency as a rule come to
the conclusion that they are fools and
wish they had followed the good old
custom of winning: the affections of girls
Whose antecedents were known to them.
Bain's Matrimonial Agency in this city
has been the means of many heartaches
and worse, and now the female head of
the concern has been arrested at the In
stigation of a dupe who has the courage
to brave public ridicule.
About the beginning of January last
H. C. Franks, a wealthy miner in El
Paso, Tex., noticed an advertisement in
Bain's paper, "The Marriage Bell," that
a widow worth $250,000 wanted a husband;
money no object, but only he must be
congenial. He thought over the matter,
and being a bachelor he decided to write
to the address in the advertisement. In
a few days he received a reply from
Mrs. Grace Allison, who is the female
head of the bureau, that she was pleased
with his letter, and begging for further
particulars about Hfta and his anteced
ents. He did not like the tone of the
letter, but replied and received a reply
that he appeared to be the man she had
for years been looking for.
Franks found it necessary to go to New
York on business, and when he reached
Jamestown, N. V., he sent Mrs. Allison
a diamond engagement ring worth $110.
After he had finished his business in
New York he came to this city and met
Mrs. Allison, who was then living at 516
Jones street. He was delighted with her
and it was agreed that they should be
married this month. She showed Franks
a number of houses that she said belonged
to her, and he thought himself the lucki
est man on earth. He remained in the
city from February 3 to 13, when he left
for El Paso.
Before leaving she told Franks that she
would like to have some token of his love
and ho might send her some silk for
dresses and some of the valuabk- stones
that were to be found In Teas. He sent
her 128 yards of silk valued at $1 50 per
yard, and opals and other precious stones
altogether of the value of about $1000.
All this time they wore corresponding
in the most endearing terms, and Franks
longed for the day when he could call
the wealthy and fascinating widow his
own. He arrived here a few days ago
and called upon her to fix the day for the
wedding. She was cold in her manner
and urged him to return to El Paso as
she was afraid some bewitching young
maiden might fascinate him and he would
forget her. He reminded her that their
marriage waa to take place this month,
but she begged that the ceremony be
postponed a short time longer.
She had occasion to leave the room and
Franks" attenlion was attracted by a
pile of letters on top of a bureau. He
was curious and examined them and was
surprised to find that they were answers
to the "wealthy widow" advertisement.
He got suspicious and asked her what the
letters meant and she put him oft with
laughing reply. He went to Attorney
Humphrey and explained to him the cir
cumstances, and he called upon Mrs. Al
lison Wednesday evening. The attorney
demanded the return of the engagement
ring, the silk and the precious stones, but
Mrs. Allison advised him to tell Mr.
Franks to go to a warmer climate than
California.
Yesterday morning Franks reported the
rase at police headquarters and a de
tective wm sent to 516 Jones street to
bring Mrs. Allison to Chief Lees. She
;iad fled, but It waa ascertained later that
she had taken rooms in a house on Hyde
street, between Union and Pacific streets.
A warrant was sworn out for her arrest
on the charge of grand larceny by trick
and device before Judge Joachimsen, and
Sergeant Moffltt was detailed to arrest
her. He succeeded in doing so last night
and she was locked up in the City Prison.
"Do I look like a thief? Would any one
take me for a criminal?" exclaimed the
prisoner last night. "Why, I am a woman
of wealth and refinement. That man."
pointing to Desk Sergeant Brown, "has
taken nearly $4000 in diamonds from me.
It's an outrage that I am placed in this
vile place. 1 was walking to my home
when a man accosted me and asked me
whether I was Mrs. Allison. I answered
'How dare you address me' How dare
you speak to a lady?' With that he
grabbed me by the arm and says 'You
come with me.' Of course I could not
resist, and went with him.
"I had an Inkling that this man Franks
contemplated my arrest, but I did not
think he would go so far. Why, I can
prove that every present he gave me was
a gift, and how he can charge me with
theft is something I don't understand.
He has been sending me flowers since
his arrival In the city. He owes a florist
$18 for flowers sent me up to a few days
ago.
"That man Is angry at me because 1
did not loan him $10,000. A few days ago
he tried to borrow $7. r > from me, but I
refused him. He said he was erecting a
smelter, but I knew he was not, because
he had no money, and bad no business
ability to do it. Mr. Franks is lacking
In intelligence and refinement, and in fact
is not my social equal. I have hundreds
of letters from him in my room, showing
his affection for me. I never cared for
him and would never marry him, but if
a man forces his gifts on a woman she
cannot refuse to accept them.
"I made his acquaintance through the
marriage bureau. He was in Colorado,
but after we became acquainted he came
out here to marry me. As he did not
come up to my expectations, 1 put him
off."
SHOT IN THE BACK.
Manual Meads Shoots Fred Miller in
a Quarrel.
Fred Miller, night cashier in the Brook
lyn Restaurant, at 207 Sixth street, and
vho resides at 969 Howard street, was
shot in the back last night by Manual
Meads, a brother of John Meads, the pro
prietor of the restaurant. He was ar
rested by Officer T. P. Riordan and a
charge of assault to murder placed
against him. Young Miller was removed
to the Receiving Hospital, and although
Dr. Hartley probed for many minutes he
was unable to extract the bullet.
Miller claims that Meads entered the
restaurant last night and charged him
with bearing false reports to his brother.
This the wounded man denied and em
phasized his denial with a few choice ex
pressions. Meads then smashed him in
the face and a fight resulted. Miller had
his enemy on- the floor pummeling him
when Meads pulled a gun out and shot
at him.
Friends then separated the two, but
when Miller was moving to his desk
Meads shot him in the back.
The prisoner has charge of the Cream
ery Restaurant, 117 Sixth street, also
owned by John Meads.

Burned While Heating Alcohol.
Joe Leckner, a young boy employed In a
liquor store at 416 Pine street and residing
at 4 Hunt street, was severely burned
about the face and hands while heating
alcohol. He was treated at the Receiving
Hospital.
